 On the History of the Text The Spaniard Alphonso, former Bishop of Jaen, prepared the first edition of the Revelations of St. Birgitta. His work was based on the Latin translations of Birgitta's Swedish text made by her Swedish confessors, the most important being the Prior of Alvastra, Petrus Olavi. In 1377 Alphonso's edition was com- pleted and could thus be laid before the commission appointed by Pope Gregory XI during the proceedings to canonize Birgitta undertaken that same year.1 Different opinions have been advanced about the history of the revelation books; much of the discussion dealt with the form and contents of the Alphonso edition.2 On the following pages I intend to examine what has been said in the discussion of the fifth book, the so-called Liber questionum, in this present volume for the first time published in a critical edition, based on an investiga- tion and an evaluation of the manuscripts. The point at issue in the first part of this chapter is the question of when the Liber questionum was first called "the fifth"; thus I shall only discuss a small part of the great problem of deciding, when the large collection of Birgittine revelations was divided into books with a fixed order. The question of the contents of the fifth book and their arrange- ment will be dealt with further on in this chapter, in connexion with an investi- gation into the relationship between the Latin text and the Old Swedish one. H. S chiick and K. B. Westman held the opinion that the revelations had already been divided into seven books, when Alphonso started his editorial work. S. Kraft on the other hand claims that this division could not have been made during Birgitta's lifetime.3 According to Kraft 61, a wholly convincing argument is to be found in the Vita written by the two Swedish confessors and handed over to the Bishop of Spoleto, Galhardus, in December 1373, at Montefalco, when Birgitta's friends passed through the town on their way back to Sweden with her body.4 The passage referred to by Kraft is A&P 86 (in.) Quid plura? Testimonia enim tantarum virtutum, tante sanctitatis et excellencie, gracie tam affluentis et habundantis, diuinitus in ea refulgentis pandunt libri celestium reuela- cionum diuinitus sibi datarum, liber eciam questionum, qui eciam ea equitante et jtinerante ad villam Wastenam diuinitus sibi datus est infusione a spiritu sancto Cf. Bergh 17. Cf. Bergh 18. 3 See Schack 19 f.; Westman 23,44; Kraft 59 ff. Rev. VIII ("Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges"), which is generally accepted as having been edited by Alphonso (cf. Westman 25 f.), and other texts apart from Rev. I—VII are not taken into consideration. 4 See Ekwall 67 ff. 1 2 
 
 
 
 
 4 miro modo quasi in vna hora, vt in principio eiusdem libri questionum plenius continetur. The text mentions libri celestium reuelacionum and liber questionum, and Kraft has this to say about the reference: "Här angives ju vad som senare kallats lib. V otvetydigt såsom ett från 'de himmelska uppenbarelsernas böcker' ... skilt verk, ett arbete sui generis." In other words, Kraft suggests that the Liber ques- tionum has not been liber quintus at the time when the text quoted from A&P was written.5 It is difficult to share Kraft's opinion that this distinction implicitly rules out the possibility that the Liber questionum could have been the fifth revelation book at the time when the A&P text in question was written. As the contents of the fifth book are consistently uniform, it need not be identified, as in the case of Rev. I—VII, by number alone. It is therefore by no means strange that it has been called Liber questionum: according to its prologue (§ i i) the book is founded on a single uninterrupted revelation and, on the whole, it consists of a number of questions, which Birgitta sees a monk put to Jesus Christ, and Our Lord's answers to these questions. Kraft justly describes (p. 67) the fifth book as the most uniform book within the Revelations. The meaning of the quoted A&P passage may, therefore, be "the books of Revelations and, among these, the Liber questionum as well", and it can in fact be proved that this interpretation of the text is correct. The quotation contains a reference to the prologue of the Liber questionum; the words in principio eiusdeni libri questionum cannot be understood in any other way. This seems to be Kraft's opinion too (cf. p. 61), but apparently he has not examined the prologue to which the A&P text refers. The prologue begins with these words, Incipit quintus liber celestium reuelacionum ... qui Liber questionum merito intitulatur. It is thus here clearly stated that the book is the fifth, but also that this book is called Liber questionum;6 the A&P text refers to §§ 3, 4 and i i of the prologue. To sum up, in a text that, according to Kraft, clearly demonstrates that the Liber questionum was not the fifth book during Birgitta's lifetime (she died on the z3rd of July 1373), there is a reference to another text, in which the Liber questionum and quintus liber are explicitly said to be identical. Kraft's faulty reasoning is valuable in so far as it draws attention to a passage in A&P, which provides us with a terminus ante quem for the prologue of the fifth book and, consequently, for the Latin redaction of this book as being the fifth of the revelation books. This problem might seem easy enough to solve, 5 Kraft emphasizes his opinion by declaring, perhaps rightly, a marginal note in S = Co d . Holm. A 14; fol. 34r) to be a secondary addition in the MS.: equitando ad uazstenas beata birgitta totum quintum libruni reuelacionum optinuit (A&P 86). 6 In the prologue heading, too (cf. below, p. 69), the book is called "the fifth" in most MSS., but in view of the fact that this heading appears in various forms in various MSS. and, therefore, does not provide any reliable information about the archetype, it has only limited value as an argument, whereas § i of the prologue is supported by nearly all MSS. (the word quintus has been omitted in n) . 
 
 
 
 
 15 as the reference to the prologue of the fifth book, quoted above, is part of the Vita of Birgitta, written by her two Swedish confessors and dated December 1373.7 Thus Kraft ought to have concluded from his material that the Liber questionum existed in December 1373 as the fifth book of Revelations. The solu- tion, however, is not so simple. S. Ekwall has shown that the section of the Vita containing the reference in question to the prologue of the fifth book (A&P 86), was not in fact included in the biography delivered to Bishop Galhardus in December 1373; nor did either of the Swedish confessors write this section. Ekwall compared the Vita text in Cod. Ups. C 15 with the corresponding text in A&P, pp. 73-102 ("the Process Vita"), and found that the latter contains some later additions to the text, one of which was certainly written by Alphonso, and the others probably were; see Ekwall 4o ff.8 The text which can surely be ascribed suppliciter exorando (p. 87 1.2), to Alphonso is A&P 84 (1.3) Habebat eciam so that this text contains the reference to the prologue of the fifth book. As Ekwall rightly points out (p. 51), it seems quite natural that Alphonso so referred to the prologue, since he undoubtedly wrote it; the alternative possibilities, viz. that Birgitta or one of the Swedish confessors wrote it, are excluded by § 2 sicut ipsa (sc. Birgitta) et confessores eius sepe oretenus testabantur. 9 This implies that December 1373 (as being the date when the Birgitta Vita was delivered to Bishop Galhardus) cannot be terminus ante quem as regards the Latin redaction of quintus liber. The problem is then to determine the date of the additional Alphon- sine text in the Process Vita, but this difficulty has fortunately already been overcome by Ekwall, whose arguments on p. 73 f. lead up to the conclusion that the text in question was written sometime between May 1377 and the autumn of the same year, and we can infer that before this date Alphonso regarded the Liber questionum as the fifth book of the Revelations. The dating is consistent with what is known of the publication of Alphonso's edi- tion of the Revelations: as has been said above, it was published that same year, 1377. Obviously this does not mean that the Liber questionum could not have been incorporated during Birgitta's lifetime as the fifth of the revelation books; but the facts so far known prove only that the Liber questionum and the fifth book were identical already in the first hand-written edition of Birgitta's Revelations, the one from 1377. This fact must be emphasized, since Ekwall, in spite of having furnished important evidence for the dating of the fifth book, did See A&P 73. Ekwall's investigations demonstrate, among other things, that the notarial attestations from December 1373 (A&P ror f.) have been left unaltered during the tradition of the Vita in its enlarged form, and therefore the reader of the Process Vita must be given the wrong impression that the notaries acquainted themselves with all the texts of this Vita version, and that the Vita, with regard to these attestations, already in December 1373 contained the texts that Ekwall has proved to have been added afterwards. — The problem of the C i 5 Vita had been earlier dealt with by Undhagen 83 footnote 4, and with similar results. 9 Cf. Westman 24. 7 
 
 
 
 
 16 not herself draw the conclusions of these investigations. In fact, Ekwall states (p. 85) that the Liber questionum was called "the fifth book" for the first time in Prior Petrus' deposition during the canonization process, A&P 523 (in.) liber questionum, qui est quintus in numero librorum celestium sibi reuelatorum. According to Ekwall, the book is not called "the fifth" until January 3oth 138010 thedate of Prior Petrus' deposition—but, as we have seen, the oldest known instance of that designation (before the autumn of 1377) is to be found in Alphonso's prologue to the Liber questionum. It would appear that Ekwall (like Kraft), in spite of knowing the reference in A&P 86 to the Liber questionum, simply neglected to examine the prologue of this book, referred to in the A&P passage." THE RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN THE LATIN AND THE OLD SWEDISH TEXTS Westman's and Kraft's investigations demonstrated that the Old Swedish ( = OS) version of the Revelations, published in SFSS 14 by G .E . Klemming ( = BU; 1857-84), consists almost entirely of a translation from the Latin text.12 Yet it is constantly alleged that the OS text of the fifth book at some points more faith fully represents the original. This problem should therefore be investigated; if the OS text of the fifth book, as contrasted with the others, is not based on the Latin, it is essential that this version be taken into consideration when deciding on the Latin one. The difference between the Latin redaction of the fifth book and the OS one is, first and foremost, that the reuelaciones of the book appear at different places in the Latin text, inserted among the interrogaciones,13 in the OS version all interrogaciones are put together as the first part of the book, and the revelations are set at the end (for further details, see below). Westman (p. 56) is of the opinion that the separation of the interrogaciones from the reuelaciones, typical of the OS redaction, may derive from the original arrangement. Westman expresses himself very cautiously, and further arguments are not advanced. On the other hand, he is certain that the arrangement in the Latin version cannot be original. No evidence is given, and Westman merely refers to "internal grounds". It must then be remembered, even if the Latin redaction does not reflect the original arrangement, this does not exclude the possibility that the 1, This is asserted by Ekwall in arguing against the opinion that there existed a fixed redaction of Rev. I—VII already in 1379 (see in particular p. 93); thus it seems reasonable to assume that she is not of the opinion that the Liber questionum was taken by Alphonso to be the fifth book in his edition from 1377. 11 No scholar can have been misled by any of the existing printed editions, since the book is called "the fifth" in these as weil. Information about these editions is given by Hollman 19 ff. 12 Westman 47-62; Kraft 17-42; cf. Bergh 104. 13 In the Latin redaction there are 13 reuelaciones, not 1z as Kraft wrongly states on p. 67. 
 
 
 
 
 17 Latin was the basis of the OS text version. The OS editor may, like Westman, have found the material strangely arranged in the Latin redaction and, therefore, made a simple adjustment of it. The theory cautiously put forward by Westman appears (p. 21) in Kraft's account, but here Westman's assumption has changed into a categorically for- mulated statement: "Hela lib. V uppvisar i svenskan en mera ursprunglig anord- ning än i latinet ... I ytterligare några fall är den svenska texten ursprungligare". Thus Kraft speaks of "arrangement" as well as of "text". When the problem is dealt with by B. Klo ck ar s, only the latter conception is used. Klockars (p. 33) speaks of parts of the OS text "som går tillbaka på en ursprungligare text än den latinska—särskilt slutet av Fjärde boken och hela Femte boken enligt den av Klemming tryckta texten" (cf. Klockars 78). Klockars reiterated the opinion, that the OS version of the fifth book is based upon an earlier text than the Latin one, in Ny illustrerad svensk litteraturhistoria 1,192. Kraft, Klockars and perhaps Westman, too, seem to be of the opinion that the Latin text was not the original of the OS version of the fifth book. This important question will be considered later, but we should perhaps first examine Westman's theory that the arrange- ment in the Latin redaction of the fifth book could not possibly reflect the original one. In his prologue, Alphonso (cf. above p. 15) gives an account of the contents of the Liber questionum: during a ride to Vadstena, Birgitta sees in a revelation a monk who has climbed a ladder reaching up to Heaven; the monk puts many questions to Jesus Christ, who answers them one by one. But the prologue also describes (§ i o), qualiter aliquando domina nostro virgo Maria aliqua verba eidem domine Birgitte loquebatur. These words cannot refer to the interrogaciones, all of which take the form of a dialogue between Jesus Christ and the inquisitive monk; the words must refer to the reuelaciones of the book. The word aliquando used by Alphonso, "now and then",14 seems an accurate description of the arrangement typical of the Latin redaction, whereas it can hardly refer to the OS disposition. This implies that the Liber questionum, when Alphonso included it in his edition of the revelation books, already existed with its reuelaciones inserted among the interrogaciones, as is characteristic of the Latin redaction. It does not seem probable that Alphonso would have written his prologue as he did, if he had himself arranged the material. Nor is it plausible that Prior Petrus, who translated the fifth book from Swedish into Latin,15 undertook this arrange- 14 Jfr MW s.v. 2. In Cod. Ups. C 61, the prologue text of which is based on the Latin (cf. below, p. 20 f.), the correspondence to aliquando is vndherstwndhom (p. 541). 15 Prior Petrus translated the Liber questionum according to the prologue, § 14. Cf. also A&P 523 (in.) et statim ipsa (sc. Birgitta) fecit, quod idem testis loquens (sc. prior Petrus) illa omnia scriberet, de quo factus est liber questionum. The Swedish text was thus immediately transferred to Latin, i.e. already in Sweden; scriberet in A&P must mean the translation (scrzbere in the same function A&P 9o), since the Swedish text, according to the § 14 of the prologue, had been written by Birgitta herself. Misleading information is given by Ekwall 85 footnote 14. 2 — 694476 Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 18 ment alone. The remaining possibilities seem to be either that Birgitta herself told Petrus to place the reuelaciones that way, or that the reuelaciones were inserted among the interrogaciones in Birgitta's own original. As regards the 'atter alter- native, it is as well to recall the "internal grounds" referred to by Westman (p. 56) when he suggested that the Latin redaction does not represent the original arrangement. Westman says that the reuelaciones of the fifth book cannot have had the placing typical of the Latin redaction "from the beginning". But he does not make it clear, whether he means the fifth book as it was conceived by Birgitta on her ride to Vadstena, or whether the original recording of the book is in question. In so far as the former alternative seems less probable (the problem will be dealt with below, p. 88 f.), it must be remembered that differences between these two stages of the literary genesis do not necessarily imply any contradiction, since Birgitta herself may have inserted the reuelaciones of the book: according to the prologue Birgitta was the one who originally wrote down the book, (§ 14) statim scripsit eum in lingua sua. Westman, too, is convinced that, when it was presented to Alphonso, the fifth book had its reuelaciones inserted among the interrogaciones, as is the case in the Latin redaction. But there is an anomaly in Alphonso's prologue which Westman apparently missed. Alphonso says in § I° that it is the Virgin Mary who speaks to Birgitta in the inserted revelations. This is correct only as far as the two first revelations are concerned; in rev. 3-12 Jesus Christ speaks to Birgitta and in rev. 13 it is God. Alphonso's misleading statement seems to be simply due to negligence: if he revised his statement, then he must have only examined the first two revelations; we may add that rev. 2 is placed rather far forward in the book, between int. 5 and int. 6, and rev. 3 does not appear until before int. 9. This suspicion of carelessness on Alphonso's part is confirmed by his description in § I° of the interrogaciones: among other things he says that the judge Jesus Christ answered the monk compendiose, "briefly".16 This characterization is adequate only for the first 4-5 interrogaciones; after that Jesus' answers become fairly extensive. Thus Alphonso seems here, too, to base his judgement on a cursory glance at the beginning of the book. It is of some interest to see that Lunden 2,257 wrongly translates compendiose with "utförligt", i.e. a word meaning the opposite of compendiose, but on the other hand this mistranslation indicates that Alphonso's compendiose can be considered very inadequate. In this connexion it should be noticed that Alphonso's incorrect statement that the Virgin Mary speaks to Birgitta in the reuelaciones supports my opinion, proposed above, that Alphonso was not responsible for inserting these revelations in the fifth book: if he had done so, he would in all probability have been better acquainted with the material than has proved to be the case.17 Cf. Nr. Cod. Ups. C 61 (cf. footnote 14) renders the word by ginli g a (p. 541). Thus it seems justified to be sceptical of Klockars' footnote 2 on p. 45, where she says that the prologue and some of the inserted revelations do not belong to Birgitta's Swedish period. Klockars is of course perfectly right about Alphonso's prologue, but as far as the reuelaciones are 16 17 
 
 
 
 
 19 With support of the sources it can thus be maintained that the arrangement of the reuelaciones of the fifth book, represented by the Latin redaction, may weil reflect Birgitta's own intentions; furthermore, that the redactional difference between the two text versions in no way implies that the OS one better reflects the original disposition, such as we know the OS version by BU. This view is verified by Cod. Ups. C 61, a MS. containing the OS text of the fifth book (p. 539 ff.) but not used by Klemming in his edition of BU, since he regarded the MS. as a bad exponent of the text; cf. BU 5,156. Investigations made by C.I. Ståhle and J. G u s s g a r d proved Klemming's evaluation of C 61 to be in error: as a matter of fact, C 61 represents the OS text more faithfully than do the sources adopted by Klemming in BU.18 A comparison between the Latin, BU and C 61, with regard to the redaction of the fifth book, gives the following result. In BU the text is divided into 26 different chapters, the first 14 of which correspond to the 16 interrogaciones of the Latin redaction; int. 1-3 are in BU combined in an introductory chapter. In C 61, as in BU, the interrogaciones are placed at the beginning of the book, but int. 1-3 are not integrated, and the interrogaciones are numbered as in the Latin text. Chapters 15-22 of BU correspond to rev. i, 5,6,7,9,12: 11-14, 13: 1-8 and 13: 9-23. The same texts occur, in the same order, in C 61, but in this MS. they are not numbered. The last four chapters in BU, 23-26, consist of material which BU does not have in common with the Latin redaction of the Liber questionum, viz. parts of Rev. IV 141-142, Ex. 56, Ex. 32 and Ex. 31. Only the first of these four concluding BU chapters appears in C 61, i.e. a text corresponding to parts of Rev. IV 141-142 according to the Latin redaction. This text is not numbered in C 61, but of still greater interest is the fact that the text was written not in immediate connexion with the fifth book but after a vacant space of more than seven pages, just before the sixth book (p. 647 ff.). It seems reasonable to associate this with the Latin text's lack of correspondence to the four chapters of BU (23-26). In other respects, too, C 61 resembles the Latin more than does BU. In C 61 the words the andra reuelacio are written before int. 6 (p. 552), i.e. in a place where rev. 2 is to be found in the Latin redaction. The words reveal familiarity with the Latin disposition and are written by manus prima. The same hand has after int. 9 (p. 572) written ffyaerdhe reuelacio ok x sporielsse; in the Latin text rev. 4 has its place concerned (with the exception of the headings), I cannot see anything to disprove my theory that they were all to be found in the Liber questionum already at the time when the text was translated to Latin; cf. above, footnote 15. Cf. also Rev. VII prol. § 3 ... in libro questionum in vltima reuela- cione sibi data in regno Suecie... , where Rev. V rev. 13: 23 is intended.  20 between int. 9 and int. 10. These notes in C 61, like the above mentioned simi- larities between the Latin and C 61 as compared with BU, may in theory be explained as caused by contamination of an OS and a Latin text,19 but in view of Ståhle's and Gussgard's investigations, which demonstrated the better quality of the C 61 text in comparison with BU, it is more natural to regard the resemb- lances between the Latin version and C 61 as indicating that the OS text followed the arrangement of the Latin at an earlier stage of the textual tradition than the one represented by C 61 and BU. Westman's idea, criticized above, that the redactional difference between the Latin text and BU may imply that the latter is closer to the original (see above, p. 16), will of course lose its justification, if this difference proves to be an illusion induced by BU; the following investigation will solve the problem. While the theory, proposed and justified above, that the OS text of the fifth book was, at some time, arranged in the same way as the Latin, obviously allows for one text having been translated from the other, it does not imply any indication of which text was the original. With regard to what has been said so far, the extant Latin text may be a translation from an OS text from an early stage of the OS tradition, a stage not known to us by any extant MS.; this OS text, then, appears with differing degrees of corruption in C 61 and BU. But the Latin text may weil have been the original of the OS version. If this latter alternative is correct, it is possible that the differences between the Latin and the preserved OS text arose at the translation stage. Then the earlier redactional stage of the OS text, assumed above, did not exist in the sense of a text written in OS; it must, in accordance with this hypothesis, have been written in Latin. The follow- ing comparison between the Latin and the OS text will examine, besides these two alternatives, a third possibility, viz. that the two text versions are derived from different sources. The OS text is quoted according to BU with an indication of the corresponding C 61 text.20 Divergent readings in the latter are not noted unless they are considered to be of some interest. As far as the prologue text is concerned, it is quite clear that the Latin is primary, at least in relation to the C 61 recension. It has already been said (p. i 5) that the prologue must have been composed by Alphonso, and as C 61 presents a text (pp. 539-542), which corresponds to the Latin almost word for word, then it must have been translated from the Latin. Other explanations imply either the untenable idea that Alphonso wrote his prologue in Swedish, or the very unlikely presumption that the extant Latin prologue is translated from an OS text, which in turn was translated from Alphonso's Latin text. Furthermore, some of the few differences between the Latin and C 61 are most significant. As for the short prologue in BU (2,273,1-8), all the information given by the BU text is to be found in the Latin and in C 61 as weil, whereas the latter two sources contain much that is lacking in BU: BU does not have any text at all corresponding to §§ 2-6 and 11-12. The Latin priority of the prologue text does not necessarily lead to the conclusion that the whole OS text of the fifth book has been translated from the Latin. What then is to be said of the differences between the Latin and the OS text in the interrogaciones? Now and then the OS text has one word too many in relation to the Latin, very often an added synonym. The quotation above from BU 2,299,24 ff., for instance, contains the word andre without any Latin equivalent. Int. 7: 13-14 is a similar case, where nobilibus is twice rendered by ärlicom foräldrom (BU 2,283,19. 22; C 61, p. 559). Such cases tell us little about the relationship: either they are emissions in the Latin, or they are meant to be elucidatory additions in the OS text.23 It seems suitable to assign to this cate- gory int. 9: 25-26, where the word insensibilia once corresponds to trä o c anno r tholik thing (BU 2, 290, 23; C 61, p. 571), and on another occasion to b ä r g h o c the ra handa (BU 2, 290, 27; C 61, p. 571); but since the OS expressions quoted may be regarded as due to an intention to clarify the text, they support, in fact, the Latin priority. On the other hand, there are many passages, sometimes comparatively long, extant only in the Latin, with no corresponding text in the OS version. Furthermore, it seems to be out of the question that the Latin trans- lator could have made these additions, if the Latin text was copied from the OS, at least such as we know this OS text from C 61 and BU. The OS text version may be cited: int. 3: 4 25 A later hand in C 61 has added, at the bottom of the page, the following words: S as om scriffuit er Jach gud lather korna all (all ex allom corr.) droffwilse. Klockars explains the excess Latin words as having been added in the Latin in relation to the original text (p. 78), using the argument that these words are not necessary for the comprehension of the text. Now, it must be considered a dangerous practice, from the methodological point of view, to cast suspicion upon "unnecessary" words when judging the authenticity of a text. Since corrections and additions are very often made in the tradition of a text in order to facilitate the understanding of it or to otherwise make it clear (see below p. 3o), it seems, in fact, more natural to regard such material as authentic as cannot have been added on those grounds. In addition, the words Ego sum Deus creans malum in the Latin text may very well have been taken to be contrary to the general teaching of the Church ("den teologiskt mycket diskutabla Vulgata-översättningen", Klockars 78), and therefore their deliberate omission from the OS version is quite understandable. The correction in C 61 is of some interest: it is obvious that the corrector, for some reason, found something to be lacking: nevertheless he avoided giving an OS correspondence to the words (Ego sum Deus) creans malum, though the original of the correction in all probability contained these words.—During the translation work at Birgitta' revelations in Vadstena, some passages were evidently censored, as shown in Cod. Holm. A 5 a (written in Vadstena), which omits from the translation certain chapters dealing with bad priests. Some of these chapters, however, are given in Latin, and the editor explains his mode of procedure by means of two different arguments, the first of which is somewhat far-fetched and intended for readers without knowledge of Latin, whereas the second is more sincere and meant for the Latin readers: Här näst six nak at af f p re- stomen ok är thetta ey stor nytto älla kännedombir lekmannom at vita, ok klärkanne älskä meer at vita ok läsa latino thy for thera skuld scrifwas thässo nästo capitula a latino; then the Latin heading follows with these words added: et ponitur hic in latino ne Clerus skandalizetur a laicis et dampnetur (see BU 5, 146 f.; cf. Westman 52). 25 A later hand in C 61 has, at the top of the page, added some words that, unfortunately, are partially illegible as the leaf was cut: <t>y giffz somlichom ey st<o>rr<e> gaffwor (some illegible words follow) ty (two illegible words, the second possibly gud) som <e> nsamen veth menniskiona hwg Jach veth och giffwa myna <n>ad oc gaffwor. 27 Some further cases are given below p. 24 f. 
 
 
 
 
 24 seems to have been deliberately altered, BU 2, 275,31 ff. (C 61, p. 546) Hwi gaff thw mannom ok qvinnom naturlik lusta j samblande om the skulo ey lifwa äptir sinom naturlicom lusta. A similar case is int. 6: 8, where the words quod conceptum est correspond to BU 2,280,7 somlik barn (C 61, p. 533, has swa som barn). In this category should obviously int. 12: 9 be included: Qui tamgin motus, ne inanis esset a fructu, Dei bonitate conuersus fuit in bonum et per diuini mandati institucionem concessum est opus commixtionis carnalis, ende natura fructificaret. The OS version here presents a more neutral and insipid text, BU 2,300,2 ff. (C 6i, p. 587) Tho vändis the rörilse aff gudz godhlek til got at naturin skulle frukt göra oc ökias. In these cases it seems reasonable to suppose that the Latin is the original; it is very possible that an OS translator—or text revisor—has deliberately distorted the text in order to avoid direct allusions to sexual matters, where an unmodified text might have been offensive,28 whereas the opposite alternative is not con- vincing, viz. that Prior Petrus or, later, Alphonso wanted to make the text more detailed and colourful in these passages. These examples from the interrogaciones demonstrate that the preserved OS text is considerably inferior to the Latin. While this difference in quality favours the priority of the Latin, the possibility cannot be ruled out that the Latin text was translated from an OS text at an earlier stage of the OS tradition than that represented by the preserved OS MSS. If an OS priority is supposed, then the errors in the OS MSS. arose in the OS tradition after the Latin translation was made.29 To sum up, the proven difference in quality between the Latin text and the extant OS recensions, of the interrogaciones, supports the Latin priority, but the evidence that the OS text was copied from the Latin one is not entirely satisfying. If we are to prove that the OS text was copied from the Latin, we must find some positive evidence of this, such as mistranslations justifying the assumption of a Latin original. Are such mistranslations to be found in the OS text? In some cases a Latin surplus text can be explained as an omission propter homoeoteleuton in the OS text, i.e. the OS translator may have jumped from one word in his Latin original to another identical word, or a word with the same ending, and therefore neglected to translate the words in between; such omissions may be due to corresponding defects in the Latin original used. This occurs in int. 8: 12 where the words ideo, quia excecati sunt, maledicta est creatura eorum et maledicta sunt ydola ipsorum correspond to BU 2, 286,23 f. (C 61, p. 564) Thy äru the blinde oc thera affgudh forbannadh. The theory of omission propter homoeoteleuton is also applicable in a case like int. 13: 35 Cf. footnote 25 as to the question of intentional alterations; cf. also Hollman 6o f. Errors may of course have arisen within the OS text tradition. An example of text corruption that would imply a Swedish written original is int. 15: 37 Sic et Iob probatus est. The corresponding words in C 61 are, quite correctly, swa pr offwadis iob (p. 618), whereas BU 2,317,28 f. has the sensational error Swa pröfwadis jak. 28 29 
 
 
 
 
 25 ad humiliacionem eorum et erudicionem, quandoque propter ingratitudinem et temptacionem, quandoque propter nature defectum, to compare with BU 2,307, 15 f. (C 6i, p. 600) til thera ödhmiukilsa ok lärilsa ok stundom for naturinna vanskilse. In these two instances the OS syntax was adjusted by the addition of o c (ok). Another example occurs in int. ii: 14-15 Primo propter humani corporis debilitatem, cuius substancia est terrea. Si enim corpus alicuius hominis videret deitatem, llquesceret sicut cera ante ignem, with corresponding OS text in BU 2,297,1 f. (C 61, p. 582) först fore licammans krankdom hulkin som smolte swasom vax fore elde (j eldhenom C 6i). The OS syntax was here regulated by means of hulkin som; the Latin cuius was perhaps significant in this connexion. The value of these passages as proof, however, is weakened by the fact that the theory of homoeoteleuton is appli- cable, particularly regarding the first two cases, if the errors arose within the OS tradition; another argument against their value as evidence is the large number of OS omissions in general. An interesting case, where a suspicion of an OS mistranslation is legitimate, is int. Io: 31 Omnis, qui viam nouam condere vel inchoare voluerit, necesse est, vt ipse conditor et inchoator eiusdem vie alios precedat. This sentence corresponds to BU 2,294,26 f. (C 61, p. 578) Hwar then som vil tee ällir byria nyan vägh äghir foregaa then sama väghin. The OS text is somewhat shorter, but the interesting point is the use of the word te e, which must answer to the Latin condere. The following conditor makes it clear that condere is correct (cf. inchoare—inchoator in the same paragraph). The OS tee cannot have the same meaning as condere, while it is often used in the OS text as equivalent to the Latin ostendere, for instance int. 2: 9 (275,12; 545),30 int. 5: 8 (278,9; 55o), int. lo: I I (292,13; 574), int. lo: 17 (293,8; 575). A simple way of explaining te e in int. lo: 31 assumes that the OS translator saw, or thought he saw, not condere, but ondere, which he took to be an abbreviated ostendere. (For the abbreviation ond-, with some abbreviative sign, for ostend-, see Cappelli 250 f.) The two principal prerequisites of this explanation seem reasonable enough: the first letter of the word condere passed unnoticed by the translator, perhaps because it was indistinctly written, and furthermore, the translator assumed an abbre- viative sign in his original, which the latter, in accordance with the theory of mistranslation, probably lacked.31 Alternatively, there is the possibility that the Latin MS. which was used, wrongly had ostendere instead of condere; in this case mistranslation is out of question, but the OS text is still bound to the Latin. This explanation would seem quite plausible, but as a matter of fact, at least one argument can be found against it: the word tee could be explained as an intentional correction of the text. In that case the corrector based his correction 30 31 The references within brackets concern BU 2 and page in C 61 respectively. This latter type of error is not unusual; it can be exemplified by Rev. VI 52 G and 63 B: in both places the MS. K has tamen instead of the correct tu. 
 
 
 
 
 26 on a later passage in the same interrogacio, § 34 vt ... via ... ad celum ostenderetur =BU 2,295,3 ff. (C 6i, p. 579) ther til at ... väghir (skulle) tees (tees in marg. C 61) til himerikis, and he considered that the same idea ("show a way") is involved in § 31; this possibility cannot be completely ignored. A passage of great interest is int. 12: 17 Verum eciam iusticia deitatis mee fuit, vt misterium incarnacionis diuine absconderetur dyabolo et hominibus tempore gracie reuelandum. The corresponding OS text is BU 2,300,32 ff. (C 62, p. 588) ok var thz mins guddoms rätuisa at mina fötzlo ämbite (mina fötzlo ämbite] myn (ex mynae corr.) fodilse C 6i) skulle lönas fore diäf- lenom ok opinbaras mannom j nathinna tima. In the OS version two finite forms were co-ordinated in the subordinate clause, skulle lönas and (skulle) opinbaras respectively, whereas the Latin has only one finite form, absconderetur. This syntactic difference is very easy to explain as a mistranslation in the OS text: the translator took et to co-ordinate, not dyabolo and hominibus but absconderetur and another finite form. In fact, Lunden 2,283 makes the same mistake: "Det var min gudoms rättvisa, att den gudomliga mandomsanammelsens mysterium skulle döljas för djävulen och i nådens tid uppenbaras för människor- na". If the Latin text is translated normally (cf. Hofmann-Szantyr 374), i.e. if reuelandum is taken to be an adjunct of misterium, no contextual complications need arise; on the contrary, the meaning ("be hidden from the Devil and from men, to be revealed in the time of grace") will be in harmony with other passages in the same interrogacio, cf. § 21 Item, cur non ostendi hominibus natituitatem meam, respondeo etc. and § 22 ideo abscondendum erat misterium pietatis mee dy ab olo, quia occultus venire volui ad debellandum dyabolum. God's incarnation is intended throughout; it was hidden according to § 21 from men, according to § 22 from the Devil. Since it is consistent with Birgitta's thinking to co-ordinate dyabolo and hominibus (§ 17), we must, consequently, assume an OS mistrans- lation. In the same passage, int. 12: 17, there are very good reasons to take the OS ämbite as a mistranslation of misterium in the sense of ministerium instead of mysterium.32 The same is true of int. 12: 22 abscondendum erat misterium pietatis mee dyabolo, quoted above, with the corresponding OS text in BU 2,301,25 f. (C 6i, p. 590) lönte jak mins mildoms (mandomps C 61) ämbite fore diäflenom. As has been said above, Birgitta is speaking of God's incarnation and how it is hidden from the Devil, cf. Lunden 2,284 "... skulle ... min mildhets mysterium hemlighållas för djävulen". There are, in fact, several Medieval Latin instances of misterium = ministerium,33 and to them may be added Rev. VI 58 A quomodo etiam ministeria angelorum apparuerunt (cf. Matth. 4,11; Marc. 1,13): ministeria is the reading given by the Ghotan edition, but of the five MSS. that 32 The original of C 61 probably had the same reading as BU, as is indicated by the primary mynae in C 61: the copyist had to correct that to myn, since he let fodilse (and not ämbite) be the subject of the clause. 33 See F. Blatt in ALMA 4, 8o ff. and different dictionaries, e.g. Ai, Be 9, Lm and NG1. 
 
 
 
 
 27 I have examined so far, only one, K, has ministeria, the others have misteria (r B 15 F). The lack of original OS examples of ämbite =mysterium (`mystery'), at least as far as can be seen from Söderwall 2,1105 ff., makes a theory of mistranslation most plausible, but as I have pointed out elsewhere,34 there is a theoretical possibility of explaining the striking ämbite in these cases in another way. The OS ämbit e, apparently influenced by ämbit e as rendering Latin officium in expressions such as diuinum officium =gudhelikit (gudz) ämbite,35 has been used in the sense of `sacrament', particularly 'the Eucharist'; since ämbite had this meaning in common with mysterium,36 it is not impos- sible that the word, by means of a semantic expansion, has taken over other meanings of the word mysterium, e.g. the principal sense `mystery', `secree. From this point of view, ämbite in our two cases need not be a mistranslation, although it probably is.37 There is another passage in the interrogaciones where the difference between the OS text and the Latin is most easily explained as a mistranslation in the OS version, int. 13: 33 Multociensque natura patitur defectum, quando contra naturam conatur et peccatur. In Westman's opinion, the arrangement typical of the OS redaction (with the interrogaciones placed together in the beginning of the book) may prove that the OS version is closer to the original text, but my investigation indicates the falsity of this assumption: the redactional difference arose when the book was translated into OS, or within the OS tradition; cf. above, p. 2o. There is nothing to prevent SA from having been translated earlier than the other revelation books; considering the important röle that SA played in the daily monastic life (cf. Klockars 109), it is hardly surprising that a translation of this text was deemed more urgent than of other revelation books. Another text, Reg. Salv., was translated to OS com- paratively early, in order to satisfy a practical need in the monastery of Vadstena: according to Gussgard 90, a translation of one of the Rule versions already existed before 1378-79, perhaps before 137o. Since Birgitta is repeatedly called sancta in the OS version (cf. above, p. 21 with footnote 21), and since this designation, used in this way without any reservation, presupposes her canonization in 1391 (cf. Bergh 86 with lit.), it seems all the more important not to date the whole OS version by means of the SA text in Cod. Holm. A im We may add that neither Birgitta's name nor any other designation for her occurs in the SA text of A 110, while sancta (used about Birgitta) can be instanced in other parts of this MS. (e.g. foll. 235', 236r and 247r, in a version of the Birgitta Vita). In consequence, it would be more correct, at present, to take the year 1391 as a terminus post quem for the OS version of the revelations, N. Even if BU in this case really reflects the archetype of the OS tradition, the additional OS text in question cannot be regarded as a particularly significant obstacle to the assumption that the OS text has been translated from the Latin; it would seem that the words were added for clarity, probably in the BU recension and not in the OS archetype. The same is true of the rest of the chapter, BU 2,332,15-333,32, answering to Rev. IV 142 from the last third of the paragraph A in Gh onwards to the end of the chapter. The OS version obviously has a worse text of Rev. IV 142 D Et quamdiu patiar te inobedientem, tamdiu prosperaberis (Lunden 2,246 "Så länge jag tål dig, som är olydig mot mig, så länge skall du ha framgång"), as compared with BU 2,333,23 if. 0 huru länge skal jak thola thik olydhoghan älla huru länge skal thw framgang haua. At the beginning of the sentence the OS translator took quamdiu to be interrogative, and using äll a he constructed two co-ordinated interrogative clauses; or else he read, in his original text, quamdiu instead of tamdiu. The result is a sentence, which misses the point of the Latin. Summary The texts that constitute the fifth book of the OS redaction of Birgitta's revela- tions have been translated from the Latin. The opinion, held by earlier scholars and expressed in different ways, that the OS edition of the fifth book contains a more accurate version of the original than does the Latin, is proved to be erroneous. Furthermore, it is demonstrated above that the arrangement of the book, repre- sented by the Latin redaction, with 13 reuelaciones inserted among the interro- gaciones, in all probability corresponds to Birgitta's own intentions. The disposition adopted in the extant OS MSS., which differs from the Latin arrangement and gave rise to the mistaken theory of OS priority, arose when somebody, perhaps the translator himself, collected all the interrogaciones together in order to facili- tate a survey of the central contents of the book; it is possible that such an arrange- ment was considered to correspond to the original recording. It can be shown by different examples that Birgitta's texts were comparatively freely handled at Vadstena.48 As far as the OS translation is concerned, this freedom is indicated by the alterations pointed out on p. 23 f. In addition, during the editorial work on different Latin texts, a certain independence from the Latin originals seems to have existed; cf. Bergh 88 footnote 18. There is no reason to pay as much attention to the OS text when editing the Latin text of the Liber questionum as would have been necessary, if the OS MSS. had contained a truer version of Birgitta's own text. In the light of these findings, the OS text is of secondary interest, in so far as it demonstrates how the Revela- tions, written in Latin, were interpreted in Vadstena at the end of the 14th century. Unfortunately we must assert that at many points Klemming's edition gives a misleading picture of this OS text, such as we can reconstruct it by means 48 Cf. Westman 52,72 and my footnote 25 above. 
 
 
 
 
 33 of a comparison between the Latin and the OS text handed down in Cod. Ups. C 61. It must, therefore, be considered to be very urgent that the OS text be edited once more, with a rightful exploitation of the important MS. C 61;49 cf. Ståhle 3o. 49 Klemming's negative appraisal of C 61 is a very illuminating example of the risk of underesti- mating later sources (cf. BU 5, 154 ff.); as far as the principal question is concerned, see Maas 31 f. ("recentiores, non deteriores"). Klemming makes the same error of judgment in discussing Cod. Holm. A 5 b (contemporary with C 61; see BU 5,154). Though this MS. is fragmentary (the fifth book is lacking), it is nevertheless considered by Ståhle (p. i) to be the most important source of knowledge about the oldest Swedish translation of the Latin redaction of Birgitta's revelations. 3 — 694476 Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 The Manuscripts In the introductory section of my critical edition of Rev. VII, I examined, classified and evaluated 45 manuscripts. The tradition of these MSS. was found to fall into two branches, derived from a common archetype which was not identical with the original codex of the Latin redaction. One of the two branches of the Rev. VII tradition is represented by 17 MSS. and designated p, while the rest of the MSS. form the other branch, designated t. Furthermore, the p MSS. were shown to consist of two groups, called (31 and [3 2; the latter contains a sub- group, e, formed by seven MSS. The n MSS., too, fall into two groups, y and a; as regards the MSS. of the group a, however, the material available to demonstrata their origin from a common source was considered weaker than in the case of the other MS. classes. Since nearly all the MSS. containing the Liber questionum include Rev. VII as well, it seemed natural to take the results gained in the study of Rev. VII as the basis of the investigation into the Rev. V tradition, and then examine such MSS. as were not dealt with in the Rev. VII investigation. The following method was adopted. From the 21 MSS. which were used for the establishment of the Rev. VII text (cf. Bergh 112), I singled out good representatives of the groups X3 1 , p2, y and a. From pi l took both r and Y (cf. Bergh 5o), from p, B m Oa and from the e group only 0, considering this MS. the best exponent of the E text (cf. Bergh 48). From the y group I chose only one MS., n, since this MS. was found to reflect the y hyparchetype better than the other MSS. of the group: in the Rev. VII investigation, n alone constitutes one of the two branches of the y tradition (cf. Bergh 29 ff.). Finally, from the a class I took four MSS., P F K q; this may seem to be many as compared to only one MS. from the y group, but the pro- cedure is justified by the difficulty, mentioned above, of proving a common origin of the a MSS. in the Rev. VII tradition. All these eleven MSS. were com- pletely collated, their relationship was established, and a comparison was made with the Rev. VII tradition. After the publishing of Rev. VII and during my work on Rev. V, I have been informed of some MSS. previously not known to me. These MSS. were not completely collated, since partial collations in combination with tests taken at random enabled me to decide upon their place in the tradition and their textual quality. Some of them were found to be useful for the edition and were therefore later completely collated. The method used implies that all the known Liber questionum MSS., with 
 
 
 
 
 35 the exception of Rev. VII MSS. of inferior quality, have been examined com- pletely or partially. In theory there is a certain risk that MSS., which were judged to be of inferior quality in the Rev. VII tradition, could be good MSS. in the Rev. V tradition; that different parts of a MS. are not necessarily derived from the same original, will, in fact, be exemplified in the following investigation.1 However, it does not seem very likely that the MSS., which were not taken into consideration, could really affect the reconstruction of the archetype; it also appeared from occasional checks in some of these MSS. (X w x, and V) that their place within the tradition is identical in Rev. VII and Rev. V. I . p--7T A systematic analysis of the whole Liber questionum gives many cases, in which one reading occurs in YBOm0a (=p), while another is found in F K n q r (Gh) ( =n).2 Two important differences from Rev. VII are already noticeable here. One concerns r, which in the majority of the cases has the same reading as n; in Rev. VII, r is a representative of the f3 family (Bergh 33 f.). In the following account r is, therefore, included in the sign n. The other MS. differing from Rev. VII is P. In Rev. VII this MS. was ranged in the a group (Bergh 52 ff.), but in Rev. V it very often joins the 13 MSS.; the P reading will be listed separately in each of the following passages. In many of the cases of difference between p and n, it proved to be impossible to eliminate either reading as erroneous on contextual or linguistic grounds, but for some of them arguments of that kind may be advanced. In int. i o: 14 Hoc igitur vinum, quod sum ego ipse, ideo ingrediebatur viscera virginea, vt ego Deus inuisibilis lierem visibilis, vt homo perditus liberaretur, the pronoun quod is omitted from p P. The f P text here seems a little odd, since it has already been sai d misit that Jesus Christ is intended by the word vinum, § II Deus Pater meus vinum suum, idest me filium suum; in the n MSS. the explication of vinum is inci- dentally repeated in § 14 by means of a sub-ordinate clause, which is contextually more appropriate. If the p P text is nevertheless authentic, then it is necessary to ask why this quod was added in the other MSS. It is very probable that a sentence without quod was considered incorrect in so far as ingrediebar would have seemed more natural than ingrediebatur. This idea is, in fact, supported by the correction ingrediebar which was made by a second hand in B and by manus prima in Oa. In view of this it appears less likely that quod is due to correction, and, in consequence, the absence of quod is an error binding p P together. Another case of common error in the p, MSS. is to be found in int. 12: 19 Admirabilis eciam fuit ibi potencia, quia ego Deus incorporalis egrediebar de vtero corporalis salua virginitate. Instead of corporalis, the reading given byn including In some of the i 5th century Columella MSS. the source MS. was changed even within a book: see S. Hedberg, Contamination and Interpolation (Uppsala 1968), pp. 5i ff. and 58 f. 2 The dependence of the MSS. upon a common archetype will be discussed in the chapter Textual Criticism, P. 74 
 
 
 
 
 36 P, the p MSS. have corporali. Obviously, corporalis as a predicative adjunct of Deus is the correct reading here. There is no point in stating that the incorporeal God went out from Virgin Mary's corporeal womb salua virginitate;3 on the contrary, such an action would be fairly trivial in view of God's incorporeality. The meaning is, of course, that the incarnate God was born, Virgin Mary still being a virgin. One may compare with SA 2: 14 Presciebat eciam se Deus in archam tui (sc. Mariae) honestissimi corporis sine corpor e ingressurum atque de ipsa cum cor por e de tua mundissima carne et sanguine purissimo assumpto egr essurum. The binding value of the f error is, however, somewhat weakened by three cir- cumstances: independently in different MSS. the ablative form corporali could be mechanically caused by the preceding ablative vtero; the followin g s- in salua admits the assumption of an haplography and, finally, corporalis in n may imply a rather easily made correction of an erroneous corporali in the archetype, handed down in the f branch. Although corporali cannot thus be judged to be an error coniunctivus, it has nevertheless been taken into consideration since it constitutes an unquestionable error. In rev. 12: 6 the text according to the n MSS. is Huic mox vt4 infundentur verba mea, liquescent perfeccius et descendent ad interiora cordis. Instead of interiora, and P here have the reading inferiora. Birgitta compares a man to a lamp, and the oil of the lamp is a simile for God's words. It seems plausible to suppose that the verb descendent is intended to indicate that God's words pass from the man's ears (or mind) down to his heart, but then it would be rather strange if Birgitta wanted to say that the words reach "the lower (lowest) parts of the heart", as is suggested by the p P reading. Some circumstances, however, might be taken to support the reading inferiora. There is a common Medieval Latin usage of infra = intra, Birgittine examples of which are listed in Hollman's glossary (further cases in Rev. VI 68, A&P 636; cf. Eranos 26,1928,269 ff. and Historisk Tidskrift 1965,152 ff.), and one may ask whether a corresponding usage of inferior = interior is to be found in the Revelations. As a matter of fact, in Eranos 8,1908,109 ff. E. Löfstedt defended inferior in some cases where editors had c hanged it into interior against the testimony of the MSS.; cf. also Svennung, Untersuchungen 366 f. Considering the well-authenticated confusion of interior and inferior in the MSS. (cf. Thes. 7: 1, 1391,2 and 2208,33 f.) it must, however, be considered 3 As for the expression salua virginitate, regarding the birth (and not the conception), cf. SA 3: 6 Presciebat vero Deus, quod in te, o castissima virgo, sine viri cooperacione debebat concipi cum honore et quod tua virginitate integraliter conseruata de te debebat honestissime nasci. 4 The use of mox ut, for the Late Latin discussed by Hofmann-Szantyr 637, is to be instanced in many cases in Medieval Latin texts, e.g. Rev. IV 25, SA 4: 18, A&P 147 and 552 (in the latter case Collijn punctuated incorrectly, joining mox to the preceding clause), Magister Mathias, Copia exemplorum, Cod. Ups. C 54 fol. 81r; cf. Strecker-Palmer 65. Statim ut is also occasionally used in the same sense, e.g. int. lo: 23, Rev. III 19 D. The tense in such clauses is generally either the perfect or the future perfect; the future simple in our case seems to be exceptional, nor was this tense used in Classical Latin in temporal ut clauses (Hofmann-Szantyr 636).

As regards our passage, I could not find any instance of inferiora cordis, whereas interiora cordis has parallels, e.g. Gaudentius, tract. 5,3 (CSEL 68, p. 44,21 ff.) ita debemus edere nostrique cor dis interioribus Huius (sc. Christi) carnem commendare, ut ... calciati simus, ut ait apostolus, in praeparatione evangelii pacis (cf. Be, s. interior 2 c). A passage which is rather similar to our case is provided by Rev. 146: 2 Verba tua, que procedunt de ore tuo, sunt michi suauia et delectant intima cor dis tamquam potus suauissimus. One further case of p error is to be dealt with here, int. 16: 7 Sed nec dextera : the f MSS. have, et sinistra in deitate mea est tamquam corporaliter effigiata instead of et (it P), the reading nec. In judging this case it seems best to begin by commenting upon Sed nec. Obviously, sed does not here have its normal adversative function; for an adversative sed with a following negative, see e.g. int. 6: 12, int. 9: 7, int. i o: 15, int. 13: 33, int. 16: 26.36 (sed non in all cases). The non-adversative sed, immediately followed by nec, has many parallels in the Revelations. In int. 16 there is another case in § 7 (sed nec feliciores sunt), and a further one in § 9 (Sed nec oues ... sunt). Other instances in the Liber questionum are to be found in int. 8: 18, int. 9: 18, rev. 4: 18, int. 11: 17, and int. 15: 12.  40 To sum up, the combination sed nec functions in the Revelations (and elsewhere) as a copulative negative conjunction without any adversative meaning of sed. Thus there is no justification for taking nec nec in the reading of int. 16: 7 as constituting a co-ordinate construction with the meaning of "neither ... nor". On the other hand, the material here presented contains cases of sed nec followed by nec (int. 15: 12, Rev. VI 32 D), and it is therefore necessary to use other arguments in deciding which reading is correct in int. 16: 7, nec or et. An interest- ing passage is Rev. VI 65 G Ego nec minimum punctum horarum et temporum expendere volo nisi in honorem dei, sed nec attendere volo pulcrum et vile, ne c vtile carnis et inutile, nec sapidum gustui meo aut insipidum. The semantically opposite notions pulcrum and vile, like vtile and inutile, are co-ordinated by means of et: in the same way there is in int. 16: 7, according to the n MSS., a co-ordination of the opposites dextera and sinistra. The last element of the sentence quoted, sapidum aut insipidum, indicates by aut that et and aut have roughly the same function in these cases, and it is of some interest to see that there also are cases of sed nec followed by a disjunctive conjunction, int. 16: 9 Sed nec oues v el edi in illa admirabili gloria mea sunt." In this last case, MS. m has, instead of vel, the error nec, apparently influenced by the preceding nec. As a matter of fact, this kind of error, viz. a nec caused by influence from a preceding nec, is not very unusual. The passage from Rev. VI 65, cited just now, provides two examples: instead of et temporum, B has nec temporum, and for et inutile there is the reading nec inutile in n (and Ib, see below, p. 61 f.). There are also cases, in which non ... et was changed into non ... nec, e.g. int. 13: 37 et ingenium instead of which MSS. m and Ab (see below, p. 59 f.) have nec ingenium. Another relevant instance of this error is to be found in Ex. 74: 2 Ego surgere volo et non parcam iuueni et seni, diuiti et pauperi, iusto et iniusto. This passage contains three examples of the co-ordination of opposite notions by means of et (cf. above), but in Lv Gh et seni was changed into nec seni. In other words, there are cases which demonstrate that the conjunction et (and vel) could in the MSS. be erroneously changed into nec when preceded by nec or non;14 in one case (the Gh text of Rev. 13 This use of disjunction instead of conjunction is dealt with by Hofmann-Szantyr 499 f., and the two cases do not exemplify the frequent Late Latin use of aut and vel = et (cf. VII 13: 24 and 23: 2. 14 Further proofs of this are given by the MSS. j Ve Eb: in spite of being derived from the n hyparchetype, these MSS., too, have nec in int. i6: 7 (see below, pp. 62 f., 64 f. and 65).—Cf. also how Hollman took vel to be the correct reading in Ex. 33: 7 vt nulla nec in moribus v el victualibus et correpcione discordia sit; for vel (Gh), the other source has nec in. On the other hand, I doubt whether Hollman made a correct choice in preferring vel in Ex. 65: 2 Deinde habeatis quatuor horas pro oracionibus legendis v el pro aliis deuotis et vtilibus operibus perficiendis. For vel the reading et is handed down in both of the two MS. groups in question (see Hollman 58); in fact, only one MS., a, of seven used for the edition of Ex. 65, has vel.  4' VI 93 A; see above), non followed by nec was changed into nec. In comparison with this, I only found one case in which an authentic nec was changed into et: in Rev. Another argument in favour of the Tr reading in int. 16: 7 has already been indicated above: the veords dextera and sinistra have opposite meanings, and illustrative passages have been adduced, demonstrating the use of a co-ordinative et (after a negative, as in our case) in such expressions. These passages (Rev. VI 65 G and Ex. 74: 2) also show how easily such an et is replaced by nec," and it seems reasonable to look upon the p reading nec in int. 16: 7 as an error of the same kind. The question of number, the singular form est, remains to be discussed. Considering cases like int. 7: i Cur vile et pulchrum dicuntur in mundo on one hand, and a case like Rev. VI 32 D Sed nec filij nec generatio illa pr osper abitur on the other, one may ask whether est in our case indicates that the p reading with nec is correct. There are, however, in the Revelations many examples of the singular form of the verb when two nouns (sometimes more) are co-ordinated by means of et, e.g. int. 7: 7 vile et pulchrum mundi est (as compared to § i, quoted above), Rev. IV 17 A Vidi, inquit (sc. Agnes), et tabescebam quia caro et sanguis puluis et stercus se ibi laudari querit vbi merito debet se humiliare. Another case is Rev. VI 116 A Jstius hominis docta insipiencia et voluntas bona sic placet; instead of placet, Gh wrongly has illacent (see below, p. 46). When the cases examined so far are taken together, they justify the assumption of a source common to the p MSS. (and P), upon which the other MSS. do not depend. Is it, then, possible to trace the n MSS., too, back to a common origin, not identical with the archetype ? In the prologue, § i i, the text runs according to P in vna et eadem reuelacione, while the n MSS. drop the word et. The p MSS. and r have the following text in rev. 8: 14: Passio quippe mea amarior michi erat quam punctura in oculo. In Y, the preposition in was later deleted, and the word is totally lacking in the n MSS. including P. The p r text punctura in penetraret and Rev. oculo would mean "a sting in the eye" (cf. § 13 oculum III 2 B secundus [sc. inimicus] stat ante eum vt pungat oculos eius; a similar phrase in IV 126 P), whereas the Y2 P n reading punctura oculo suggests a parallelism, oculo as a dative form corresponding to michi, "my suffering was more acute for m e than a sting for the eye". In Rev. VI 19, parts of which are nearly identical with rev. 8 (see above, p. 18 footnote 17), the corresponding text is (section D in Gh) Quid autem significat ocellus nisi corpus meum, cui sic amara erat (erat amara Gh) passio quasi dolor et punctura ocelli? In this latter case no parallelism is intended (ocelli in all MSS. examined), though a construction with a dative form would have been formally possible considering the preceding cui. I take this fact as an argument in favour of the p reading in rev. 8: 14. It may be objected that the genitive ocelli in Rev. VI 19 D goes against in oculo in our case, but at the same time one must admit that, if somebody wanted to modify an hypotheti- cally authentic punctura oculo, this would not necessarily lead to in oculo. In reality, the construction with the genitive and the one with prepositional attribute would have had the same function. This is supported by the readings in Aa, Wa and Fb: in these MSS., which will be dealt with below (sections 8 and lo) and there demonstrated to belong to the n branch of the tradition, oculo was changed into in oculis, in oculo and oculi respectively. As a parallel to punctura in oculo infirmus ad mortem the following case from A&P 287 (ex.) may be given, puer cum pessimo vlcere in lumbis.18. 18 Cf. A&P 458 Francisca passa fuerat ... maximum dolorem stomachi and, ibid., ipsa testis loquens paciebatur maximam infirmitatem in quodam digito. The construction in quodam digito in the latter passage is not necessarily a parallel to our punctura in oculo, since it can be adverbial; an attributive function is, however, probable, the same verb pati being used also in the former case with the genitive attribute stomachi. At any rate it is interesting to see how the genitive construction and the prepositional one seem to function in the same way in these examples.—Prepositional attri- butes, instanced in Latin texts from all periods (cf. Hofmann-Szantyr 428), are fairly common in Medieval Latin texts. The following passage from Officium Sancti Sigfridi secundum codicem Laurencii Odonis contains two cases in the same phrase, ad deum per fidem illius gentis conuersio (A. Önnerfors, Die Hauptfassungen des Sigfridoffiziums. Lund 1968, p. 92,20 f.). 
 
 
 
 
 43 In int. 16: 3, too, the f MSS. seem to have preserved the correct text, Item, cum Spiritus Sanctus tuus locutus sit in euangelistis, cur tanta est euangeliorum dis- sonancia? The preposition in is omitted from the n MSS. and P. In the responsio to this question, all MSS. have (§ i 5) Item, si Spiritus Sanctus locutus est in euangelistis, cur tanta est dissonancia etc. It is evident that § 15 repeats the phrase from § 3, and in euangelistis in § i 5 supports the p reading in § 3. With regard to the differences between p and n, it is hardly due to chance that the n MSS. have alia instead of aliqua (p and P) in int. i6: 14 Sed solus Pater ... scit horam illam iudicii, non angeli nec aliqua creatura. In this case it seems quite likely that aliqua was regarded by some copyist (or corrector) as an error: if the words non angeli nec aliqua creatura are taken to mean "neither the angels, nor any creature", it implies the false presumption that the angels were not created (cf. int. 9: 13). The p P reading need not, however, be interpreted that way. As Hofmann-Szantyr point out (p. 195), aliquis has been sometimes used in the meaning of aliquis alius (`sonst einer'). In addition to the literature referred to by Hofmann-Szantyr, the phenomenon was also studied by E. Löfstedt, Per. 174, and A. Önnerfors, In medicinarn Plinii studia philologica 216 (with lit.). As far as Medieval Latin is concerned, there is some material in MW II B i (cf. also SG1), e.g. MGH Dipl. reg. et imp. Germ., Tom. 1, p. 240,15 nec illo nec aliquo homini conficiatur servitium (from the i oth century). In this inter- pretation, int. 16: 14 non angeli nec aliqua creatura means "not the angels and no (other) creature whatever". In comparison with alia, the p reading aliqua is much more emphatic, which seems to fit in quite weil in the context, in which a certain stress is laid upon the statement that the triune God alone, as contrasted to all created beings, knows the hour of Judgment. Arguments can thus be ad- vanced in favour of the p P reading, and consequently the n MSS. are held to- gether by a common error, which was very likely caused by an intentional alteration. In some cases the difference between p and n is one of word order. Generally it must be considered difficult to decide upon such cases, and trivial transposi- tions like for instance int. I o: 32 quedam via p via quedam n P were therefore almost entirely left out of consideration in this investigation; at least it does not seem very probable that differences of that kind arose because somebody wanted to modify the text. There are, however, some cases of different word order, in which such an intention may have been of some importance, and such a case is int. i I: 5 Item appropinquante morte cur non ostendisti te in potencia deitatis tue. This is the p text; in the 7r MSS. cur is placed before appropinquante, while it is omitted from P. It is very likely that the n text implies an adaptation to the word order normally used in such questiones as contain Item and cur. Thus, the com- bination Item cur appears, before our case, in intt. 2: 3-4, 3: 2-5, 4: 4, 5: 2-5, 6: 2-5, 7: 2.4, 8: 2, 9: 2-5, i o: 3, and finally in int. II: 2-4, i.e. in the three questiones immediately preceding § 5; the possibility of influence from these passages is quite obvious. On the other hand, the p word order has good parallels in so far as a sub-ordinate clause has sometimes been placed between Item and cur as is 
 
 
 
 
 44 the case, for instance, in int. lo: 4 Dem, cum omnia gossis et vbique es presens, cur non statim apparuisti. In addition an instance not drawn from the interrogaciones also seems to be relevant, rev. 12: 14 Sic nucleus iste significat verba mea, que e x ig ent e p e cc ato cum in regno isto nunc conuenienter ascensum habere non possent, prius alibi ascendent et proficient: the absolute ablative is placed before the cum clause, though sub-ordinate to it, just as appropinquante morte in int. I I: 5 was put before the cur clause. Thus, I take the p reading in this case to reflect the archetype, whereas the n MSS. are connected by a common error, easily explained as a normalization. Another case of different word order is int. 15: 14 propter .. . cupiditatis refrenanciam humane. This is the text according to p; in n P humane was put before refrenanciam. If the difference in word order was caused by intentional alteration—a presumption which is certainly not necessary—then it seems more likely that the text was modified in the n MSS. than in p: the 7: word order may have been due to a wish to place the adjunct nearer the headword. The same is true of Prol.: i Christus Dominus mirabiles dat soluciones: here also the difference between p and n favours f3, since mirabiles is in the n MSS. and P placed immediately before soluciones, its headword. In the same category is to be listed rev. 3: 8, quia ipse ministrator et causa mortis est eorum: instead of est eorum, the reading of p, the other MSS. (including P) have eorum est, the adjunct eorum being put together with mortis. In all these cases it is characteristic of the p reading that something has been placed between adjunct and headword. Since such a word order appears in not few places in the Revelations, it is reasonable to assume that p has preserved the authentic readings, whereas n gives a normalized text. Examples supporting the p word order are: Rev. IV 139 D nulla tunc ei temporalium potestas auxiliabitur dominorum (in this passage, containing two examples of broken word order, the meaning is not that other domini than temporales will help; the placing of tempo- ralium is thus not due to any intention of emphasizing that word); VII 19: 3o graue vestrarum periculum animarum; Ex. prol. sec. v: 2 ad multarum profectum animarum, ibid. § 7 multorum decursibus annorum; Ex. 17:1 pro suarum implecione voluntatum.1-9 In Rev. VII 19: 3o there is a transposition of vestrarum periculum in P and a transposition of periculum animarum in Oa, which indicates that a word order of the kind exemplified may have given rise to modifications. Of particular interest is another case from Rev. VII, the word order being different in p and n, 7: 5 omnia sunt iam sibi indulta sua peccata; in the 7: MSS., the words sua peccata are put immediately after omnia. Just as in the Liber questionum cases the more normal word order occurs in n. In principle the assumption that the word order was repeatedly normalized in one source must be considered more likely than the opposite possibility, viz. that it was time after time rendered more complicated in another source. To these cases, which concern headword and adjunct, may suitably be added another, similar group containing cases in " For examples in another Medieval Latin text, see my article Einige Bemerkungen zur „Hystoria Tartarorum" (Symbolae Osloenses 46, 1971), p. 157. 
 
 
 
 
 45 which one or more words have been put in between two co-ordinated words, e.g. int. 2: 5 quietem habere et requiem, int. I i: 19 audiri poteram et videri, rev. ED: 6 exercitari et probari possint et a me erudiri, ibid. § 8 cecus populus Domini et surdus, int. 15: 19 subsistere poterit et roborari.20 The case rev. 3: 16 is also to be listed in this group, incendere cupiunt sed appetitum extinguere non valent; the passage Will be dealt with critically below, p. 75 f. It would seem that the word order exemplified in these cases also invited alteration: instead of int. 2: 5 quietem habere et requiem, MS. j (see below, p. 62 f.) has quietem et requiem habere, and MS. Eb (p. 65) has quietam habere requiem. Examples of the same kind of word order in other revelation books are IV 61 A tangi dignatur et amari, VII lo: 3 and 29: 5 audire spiritualiter et videre, ibid. 2o: 3 audire spiritualia et videre, SA i: 6 tangi potest vel videri; another instance of co-ordinate infinitives is in Ep. Sol. 5 E instruere nos ... voluit et docere. Very often the participles in the passive voice of the perfect are co-ordinated in this way, e.g. Decl. II 2 combustus est et mortuus, VII i i: 4 saluate sunt et liberate; a co-ordination of future participles e.g. in int. i i : 28 facturus essem et passurus. Examples can be found with substantives and adjectives, e.g. Rev. 145: 25 fortitudine vestra vos esse liberatos et sollercia, VII 15: 22 alta voce et flebili, ibid. 24: 3 ad verba eorum et actus. A case of great interest is Rev. VII 20: 23 felix essem et beatus according to the f3 MSS.; in the 7r group there is the reading felix et beatus essem. It is evident that a normalizing tendency, as far as word order is concerned, is typical of the Te recension. It should be added, as another argument in favour of the p reading in the Rev. V cases discussed, that in none of circ. 40 MSS.—i.e. not even in any p MS.—was the word order changed in Rev. VII 27: 2 audire et videre spiritualia, but on the other hand Rev. VII 29: 5 audire spiritualiter et videre was changed in Oa into videre et audire spiritualia.21 The word order is in these cases supported by 3 and Tr. Rhythmical regards may have played a röle in some of the cases discussed here, e.g. Rev. VII 19: 3o and 29: 5 (cf. Bergh 96 f.; G. Lindholm, Studien zum mittellateinischen Prosarhytmus. Stockholm 1963), but other cases do not represent any of the general cursus types at all, e.g. int. 15: 19 and Rev. VII 20: 23. Rev. IV 139 D exemplifies the cursus velox type, but it should be observed that the same cursus would have been given, even if temporalium had been put immediately before dominorum. A good parallel is Rev. VII 7: 5, where not only the p word order but also the 7r reading implies a cursus planus. It is, however, not probable that a cursus really was intended in that Rev. VII chapter, see Bergh 1.c. The same is true of Rev. VII o: 3 and 20: 3; in both cases there certainly is a cursus velox, but in all probability it was unintentional. Nor is it very likely that, for instance, the cursus velox in int. 11: 19 is due to a rhythmizing intention, since Rev. V in general shows no tendency to use the cursus technique: of 15o examined sentence endings in int. i—int. 7, only 24 were found to represent the normal cursus types (Lindholm 41, 44 and 46), i.e. 16 %. In addition, the distribution among the cursus types planus, tardus and velox is very significant: the cursus is velox in only seven cases, but it is clear from Lindholm's material on p. 198 f. that the deliberate use of cursus gives a predominance to the velox; I reached the same result in my investigation into Rev. VII (Bergh 96). Unlike the Rev. V text, Alphonso's prologue to it seems to have been intentionally rhythmized, or at least the probability of this is higher than in the case of the Rev. V text. Of 17 cases examined, there is cursus in lo; 5 velox, 4 planus and i tardus (the velox has thus the majority). 20 21

The cases confirm the theory of a common source for the n MSS. In some cases there is a word more in n, as compared to the p group. Such a case is rev. 3: 12 hic talis medicus magnam habebit mercedem a me, quia talis correpcio placet: instead of placet, n P have placet michi. It is very unlikely that a copyist considered the expression placet michi incorrect and therefore cancelled michi; no MS. omits michi in rev. 4: lo fama operum tuorum laudabilium et pulchritudo morum tuorum placuerunt michi, and the same is true of int. 13: 41 Quia litteratura non placet michi sine vita. From the theoretical point of view it seems more probable that michi was added. The problem is whether the p reading, without michi, can be supported by parallels. In rev. 4: 5 tua mundissima virginitas ... et con- tinencia omnium illicitorum motuum pla c u er u n t et fulserunt in conspectu meo cum omni humilitate, the reading placuerunt is supported by all the MSS. examined, but on the other hand it can be maintained that the following in conspectu meo may have made an expected michi unnecessary; one can compare with the Vulgate (OT) usage of placere in conspectu (or in oculis) alcis (e.g. Deut. 12,25 cum feceris quod placet in conspectu Domini, i Mac. 8,21 placuit sermo in conspectu eorum),22 which perhaps had some influence on our case. Another case of a word added in the n MSS. in comparison with the p group is int. 12: 13 ego Deus volui recipi mundissimo receptaculo. It is typical of the 7C P reading that the word celestis has the function of an adjunct of liber, and the expression liber celestis is, in fact, to be found in many places.
