Of the three MSS. Collijn used for his edition, two, L and S, have celestium, in S however corrected from celesti, which is the reading of the third source, R. If celestium is authentic here, this would cor- respond very weil to the evaluation of L, mentioned above. The R Sl reading celesti can, however, be explained in another way than as an error for celestium. As Ekwall pointed out (p. 40 ff.), the text in question (A&P 82-84) was later included in the Process Vita (cf. above, p. 15), most probably by Alphonso. Now, we know from Ep. Sol. and Alphonso's deposition in A8413 that the typical Alphonsine way of expressing "in the fourth book of the Revelations" is in quarto libro celesti; for examples, see above, p. 48. Except for the word reuelacionum, the R S1 reading celesti in A&P 83 must, therefore, be said to correspond exactly to what is known of Alphonso's usage in this respect. A suspicion as to the word reuelacionum is thus not illegitimate; it may very weil have been added in the archetype, and a case like A&P 79, cited above, could have been the model for such an alteration, but the word celesti was then not changed until later in the tradition. In my opinion, A8413 83 is to be judged in one of these two ways: either the L S2 reading celestium reuelacionum is correct, or reuelacionum is a later addition and in quarto libro celesti is the authentic read- ing, for it should be noted that neither Alphonso's deposition, nor his Ep. Sol. contains any single case of celestis used as an adjunct of liber in the phrase liber (with or without ordinal) reuelacionum. As far as the Process Vita, Dep. Cop. and the A&P edition of Codex Siena and Codex Panisperna are concerned, there is no clear case of the combination mentioned." As was demonstrated above, one case may come into consideration, A&P 510, but then it must be added that the Liber celestis, not one of the books of the Revelations (as in the case of A&P 83), is referred to in that passage. On the other hand, there are in the material several cases of liber reuelacionum celestium (apart from the combination reuelaciones celestes without liber); besides, the majority of the cases have the word order celestium reuelacionum, which proves that the word order in our case, Prol.: i, is no obstacle to the 13 reading celestium. The material does not contain any Alphonsine case of the construction type liber (with ordinal) celestium reuelacionum, but it is evident from A&P 86 libri celestium reuelacionum, quoted above, that Alphonso, too, can be said to represent the incontrovertible tendency in the material, viz. that celestis certainly can be used as an adjunct of liber, but only if the phrase does not include the genitive reuelacionum —in this latter case the adjunct "heavenly" is attributed to "revelations". It is very instructive to compare two cases already cited, A&P 561 and p. 634. These two passages contain the same text, but in vltimo libro celesti in the former corresponds to in vltimo libro celestium reuelacionum in the latter; obviously, the construction in vltimo libro celesti reuelacionum was considered incorrect (cf. my reasoning above, about A&P 83). Another striking example of this is given by a comparison between two passages in Dep. Cop., both of which have the meaning "in the fourth book of the Revelations". The difference between these two cases is significant: p. 500 in quarto libro celest i, but p. 54o in libro quarto c e les tium reuelacionum. Compared with A&P the usage found in the incipits and explicits of the different books of the Revelations is far less consistent. From examples taken at random in different MSS. 33 Ekwall 89 holds that there is an expression libri reuelacionum celestes, but apparently she is in error. 4 — 694476 Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 50 it appears that it is not only the type of construction liber (generally with ordinal) celestium reuelacionum that is represented, but also the same phrase with celestis used as adjunct of liber. Examples: Jncipit prologus in libro reuelacionum celestium F q, Jncipit secundus liber celestium reuelacionum q, Explicit fiber secundus reuelacionum celestium B, Jncipit tercius liber celestium reuelacionum q, Explicit quartus liber celestium reuelacionum n q, Incipit prologus quinti libri c ele stium reuelacionum B 0 m, Jncipiunt rubrice vltimi libri c ele stium reuelacionum n. The following cases with celestis (sing.) were noted: Jncipit liber celestis reuelacionum dei secundus B, Jncipit liber celes tis tercius reuelacionum B, Explicit liber quartus celestis reuelacionum P, Jncipit prologus quinti libri celestis reuelacionum Y, Explicit liber celes tis reuelacionum dei sextus B 0, Explicit septimus (supra lin., vitimus del.) liber celestis reuelacionum q. The cases taken from B 0 q demonstrate that both types of construction can be used in one and the same MS. This evidence could be taken to indicate that the rc P reading celestis in Prol.: i is correct. It should, however, be observed that our case from the prologue, even if the sentence begins with the word Incipit is not included in an incipit in the sense of a separate heading, but is part of the text of Alphonso's prologue. Furthermore, incipits and explicits were formed in different ways by different copyists,34 e.g. Explicit liber primus Reuelacionum and Jncipit liber secundus F, Explicit liber secundus. Incipit tercius P, Ego expleui librum celestis imperatoris ad reges m. The evidence of the incipits and explicits must, therefore, be considered weaker than the A&P material, which includes genuine Alphonsine passages. In the light of these findings, I take the p reading Prol.: i quintus liber celestium reuelacionum to be correct, though it must be admitted that there is some material in favour of the 7r P reading celestis, too. At any rate this case confirms the result given by the other cases examined, in so far as the Liber questionum tradition must be taken to consist of two branches, called f and rr; this is consistent with the situation in the Rev. The order typical of the y questiones is i, 4, 5, 2, 3, 7 and 6; the following responsiones are arranged in a corresponding way." I cannot find any good contextual argument against this order, but with regard to the errors, which bind the y MSS. together, int. 13 was obviously not arranged that way, either in the archetype or in the n hyparchetype.  55 with a n MS. made some corrector erroneously add michi in rev. 3: i z and delete in in rev. 8: 14 and int. 16: 3; the same is true of the difference in word order in Prol.: 1, rev. 3: 8 and int. 15: 14, i.e. an adaptation to the n word order is quite plausible. Another case of different word order in f and n, mentioned above, may be commented upon here, viz. int. i: 5 Item appropinquante morte cur non according to p; in n, cur is placed before appropinquante, while the ostendisti same word is lacking in P. The P reading here is very easy to explain in view of the substantiated contamination in the MS.: after a comparison with a n MS., cur was deleted in the original of P, in which MS. the word was placed as in the extant p MSS.; after that the corrector either wrote cur over the line before appropinquante (or perhaps in the margin with some sort of insertion sign), or he did not write it all; in the former alternative, the word passed unnoticed by the P copyist. This explanation is consistent with the modified word order in P which was assumed in the cases mentioned just now. The primary source of P was thus, in all probability, a p MS. With regard to the P Y errors, exemplified above, it is quite plausible to assume that P was copied from the same source as was Y. If the contamination in P was caused by corrections in its original, which seems likely (cf. footnote 41), then it is necessary to assume that the P Y source was corrected after Y was written; or, alternatively, there was an intermediate between P and the original of Y. But it also seems to be theoretically possible that the original of P was a corrected co py of Y. Obviously, the presence of n readings as weil as individual errors in P (e.g. the omission of rev. 13: 17 concepta peccato), exclude the possibility that Y was copied from P.42 The opposite alternative, however, cannot be ruled out, 43 since it is impossible to know the extension of the secondary influence in P. Considering the P con- tamination, errors in Y have no value as evidence,44 with one reservation. If the secondary source of the P text can be identified, there is in principle the possibility of establishing whether P has readings which could not possibly have been handed down from Y, nor from the secondary source, but must have been handed down from p,. Is it, then, possible to identify the secondary source of the P text ? Apparently, the secondary source of P was connected with the y branch of only in those cases, in which the secondary source was deemed to have a better text, and it is an accepted opinion that omissions, for instance, were generally not taken over in such a comparison; cf. Maas 9, Pasquali XVII, Å. Josephson, Die Columella-Handschriften 6i (with lit.). Though this theory must be considered very well-grounded, there is, of course, the risk that the comparison could sometimes be mechanical and thus lead to the incorporation of evident errors. Examples from the Columella tradition are 8,5,4 rem utentur and 8,5,5 eadendis, in both cases error in the corrected Ambrosianus; cf. Josephson pp. 42 and 93 resp ectively. 42 The reasoning includes the assumption that the Y text is not contaminated; cf. above, p. 52. 43 There are no contradictions involved as to the dating of P and Y; see Bergh pp. 36 and 55. 44 Errors in Y, besides those common with p2 (int. 12: 29 and int. 16: 7, discussed in section above, pp. 35 f. and 38 fr.), are to be found in int. 8: i est for sit, rev. 4: 20 inuitacionem (together with B) for imitacionem, and int. io: 21 omnibus for omnibusque.  56 the r tradition, and the proof of this is given by int. 13. As was said above (p. 53), the y group has a divergent order of the questiones and responsiones of int. 13, viz. 1, 4, 5, 2, 3, 7 and 6. In its questiones P has the same order as the y group. The responsiones are arranged in the same order as in p and r, but the headings of the P responsiones have an inconsequent numbering which, however, corresponds to the order typical of the y MSS., with the exception of the last two responses. In other words, while the P responsiones are presented in the numerical order 1-7, the numbers written in the corresponding headings are 1, 4, 5, 2, 3, 6 and 7. The explanation of this is quite clear: the original of P was compared with a MS. representing the y arrangement of int. 13, the difference in order was observed, and since the corrector had no reason to take the y system to be in error, but perhaps better (cf. above, p. 53), he altered the numbers of the headings, thus indicating what he believed to be the correct order. The suggested modification was carried out by the P copyist only in the case of the questiones; in the responsiones section the copyist copied his corrected original quite mechanically, with no regard to the discrepancy between numbers and actual order. There are other P y errors, e.g. possunt for int. 5: 9 possint, and the omission of int. 9: 5 ita, quoted in section 3 above. This fact is consistent with the assumed P y relationship, but it is open to doubt whether these P y errors really imply dependence, since P has errors in common with other n MSS. as weil, e.g. the omission of int. 16: 27 hoe, an error which Pi has in common with the cs MSS. (see above, p. 53); furthermore, P q1 omit int. I: 3o vt, P K omit int. 16:i et, and P has, together with r, nolui instead of int. 12: 28 volui. As regards the relationship between P and Y, discussed above, I did not find any relevant case of the kind searched for, viz. a reading handed down" to P, but stemming neither from Y nor from the y branch: a p error with an individual error in Y or, alternatively, a correct reading with errors in Y and y respectively. Only one case merits consideration, int. 9: 17, where the correct promereretur is to be found, among the MSS. of interest here, in P and X. The reading of n w x is promeretur; in Y there seems to be a correction from promeretur into pro- mereretur. However, the slight difference between these two readings and the possibility of individual alteration, exemplified in X, make this case rather in- significant. In consequence, there is no proof that P was not copied from a corrected copy of Y, but conversely there is no positive evidence whatever that it was so copied, and Y and the primary source of P may very well represent different branches of the pi. recension; "Gegen die Kontamination ist kein Kraut gewachsen" (Maas 3o). The investigation of P has demonstrated that this MS. is a representative both of P, and of a n MS. which had the int. 13 text arranged in the same way as have the y MSS. The absence of further binding P y errors admits the assumption that the secondary source of P may have been an early example of the y recension; 45 It is obvious that individual P readings are of no interest in this connexion. 
 
 
 
 
 57 theoretically, it is even possible that this source was very near the n hyparchetype. The result of the examination differs from the situation in Rev. VII in certain important respects. It is obvious that P, in the Liber questionum tradition, is dependent upon the p hyparchetype, as is shown by errores coniunctivi not only for P p but also for P Y. In Rev. VII, there certainly are some P Y errors, e.g. the omission of 13: 3o dicens46 and of 18: 3 patris vestri.46 In addition, there are some P Y r errors as we11,47, the reading persone instead of i i: R et personam, and the omission of 14:1 sponse.46 These instances in Rev. VII might possibly reflect the same relationship between P and Y as was above established for these MSS. in Rev. V, but there is the very important difference that Rev. VII in P is entirely without p errors. It would be very strange if an assumed P connexion in Rev. VII were not reflected in errors typical of the whole f group (see Bergh 33 f., 91 ff.), but only in P Y (P pi) errors, and since P has errors in common with other MSS. as weil (Bergh 59), Rev. VII would seem to require a different expianation from Rev. V. Another most important difference between the two books is that P in Rev. VII belongs to the a. group. As in Rev. V, P was also found to have y readings in Rev. VII, but the Rev. VII analysis of P did not indicate that there was a secondary y influence in P; I found it more likely that the P y readings existed in the P a source, but later during the tradition of the a text, they were altered; this is consistent with the assumed revisions of the a text, referred to above, p. 54. A combination of the results from the two investi- gations seems to be very difficult. In one case, int. 16: 27 (cf. above), the uncor- rected P has the same error as F K q (Gh), viz. the omission of the word hoc, and this may indicate a connexion between P and the a. MSS. On the other hand, this is the only case in question, and since hoc could easily be added (cf. Ioa. 2,19), it is also possible that the word was lacking in the n hyparchetype, but added in the y hyparchetype. The whole question of P is thus fairly problematic. At any rate, since the MS. does not adhere only to the p, recension, but also draws on a source which may represent an early stage of the n tradition, it would appear necessary to take it into account in the edition. 6. r Since the MS. r has nearly all the 7C errors which were discussed in section it is necessary to list it among the n MSS. There are, on the other hand, two cases of common error in r and p,, first vigilet instead of int. 8: 25 inuigilet, but this case is not likely to imply any dependence. The other case is int. 13: 4 Item cur aliis intelligencia et ingenium datur incomparabiliter docile: before docile these MSS. add et. I cannot find any convincing argument in favour of the strange et docile, whereas parallels are to be found supporting the pi n reading, e.g. int. 8: 4 incomparabiliter horribiles (sc. pene), int. i i: 16 incomparabiliter fulgencior igne et sole (sc. deitas), and SA 19: 4 incomparabiliter Salomone sapiencior est (sc. fiilius Dei). Either the error arose in p, and r independently, or it may be an, archetypal error handed down in both branches of the tradition but easily cor- rected in different MSS. (As regards the uncertain case of int. 7: 14 es instead of est, cf. the apparatus.) The placing of r within the n family is uncertain. There are som e cases of common error in r and the y MSS., e.g. genere instead of int. 7: 2 progenie and a deo for int. 16: 31 ad Deum, but it is doubtful whether these cases really reflect a relationship between r and y. The investigation of r in Rev. VII led to a different result. In that book binding errors proved r to be dependent on the p hyparchetype, as a representative of the X , branch; see Bergh 33 ff. It was also found to have been contaminated with correct readings against errors in Y p,. With some reservations I proposed the theory that the secondary source of the r text might be "Alphonso's originals" mentioned in the MS. in the explicit after the sixth book, though it was admitted that the wording of the explicit ( scriptus et correctus ex originalibus alfonsy ...) does not exactly support that interpretation: it would be more natural t o take it to mean that r was copied from a source called "Alphonso's originals", and that the same source was used when the text was corrected. It was, however, i mpossible to combine this interpretation with the important fact that the correct r readings in question do not appear in the MS. as corrections but written by manus prima. Irrespective of the correct interpretation of the explicit, it should be noted here that the theory of contamination with a primary source dependent upon the p hyparchetype, although justified in the case of Rev. VII, cannot be applied to Rev. V, because of the r n errors and the absence of r p errors.50 49 The preceding non quia and the similarity between the abbreviations of sed and licet (cf. Cappelli pp. 198 and 337) caused this error. 50 The problem of r was dealt with by J.-O. Tjäder in an incisive analysis in NTBB 55 (1968), 22 f.  59 The concluding sections of this chapter will deal with such MSS. as were not examined at all in the Rev. VII investigation, and the following method was used for establishing their place within the tradition. All the passages discussed in section i above were collated in each MS., which made it possible to determine to which branch of the tradition, p or n, the MS. belonged. After that, the errors noted in sections 2-4 in the various sub-groups were used in order to carry out the further classification. Finally, partial collations, starting at the beginning of the book gave some indication of the closer relationship between certain MSS., and the tendencies thus suggested were then examined by means of samples taken at random from different parts of the text. 7. Abc With the exception of rev. 12: 13 infigit in c, which is the correct reading whereas the p MSS. have infingit (cf. p. 37), the MSS. Ab and c have the same reading as p in all the cases examined in section i . The reading infigit in c is either a fairly easy emendation, or an unintentional divergence from infingit. Furthermore, Ab and c have all the errors listed in section 2 as typical of the p, group. Within this group Ab has readings in common with m, for instance the omission of int. 7: 23 si and of int. 8: i i exterius, the reading idest instead of int. i o: 22 et (ante tempus), and the addition of omnia after rev. 5: i tibi; another common addition is quia, which was put after int. 13: 12 Nam aut. Also the reading circumscidi for int. i o: 5 circumcidi, listed above on p. 51 as an Oa m error, appears in Ab, and to that case one may add the similar Ab m reading circumscisus for int. lo: 29 circumcisus. Individual errors in m make it impossible for Ab to have been copied from m, e.g. vanitate for rev. I: i vaniloquiis and the omission of int. 16: 13-14 Sanctus (cf. above, p. 5i). Since the opposite hypothesis seemed Nequaquam quite plausible—for instance Ab' and m have a vacant space instead of int. 7: 11 occasionem, which was later added in Ab by a corrector—I collated the whole Ab text. Further common errors were found, binding Ab and m together, e.g. the addition of tribulantur before int. 15: 46 purgantur. In only one case was Ab found to have an error not in m: after int. 9: 21 sustentacionem Ab (together with P) adds the word eius. As opposed to this single case there are great many in dividual m errors, e.g. aliquando instead of rev. 6: 5 alii, and the omission of rev. i i: R in spirituali reuelacionum. Most probably m was copied from Ab, and the o nly case working against this theory, eius (Ab) in int. 9: 21, is easily explained as an omission made in m. As regards c, there seems to be some connexion between this MS. and Oa. These two MSS. have a common omission of int. 5: 7 sanctis, and ibid. § i i "Alphonso's originals". Tjäder's theory gives rise to certain difficulties in so far as it makes two different contaminations of the p text necessary (p and (32), neither of which seems very plausible. I intend to return to this problem in my edition of Rev. VI. As far as Rev. V is concerned, it is evident that neither Tjäder's nor my theory from Rev. VII is applicable.

Aa Ma The MSS. Aa Ma have none of the erroneous p readings discussed in section i with the exception of int. 12: 19 corporali instead of the correct corporalis. The readings which were considered r errors appear in both MSS. with some exceptions: in rev. 8: 14, Aa has in oculis for in oculo p, oculo n; it has in int. 16: 3 the correct p reading in euangelistis (the Tr MSS. drop in), and ibid. § zo Aa has, like p P, diuersis locis as compared to in diuersis locis in n. Obviously, Ma should be included in the n group, and it seems reasonable also to list Aa in that branch of the tradition. The error corporali in int. 12: 19 can hardly be con- sidered binding (cf. my opinion of it above, p. 36), and the cases of correct p readings in Aa are best explained by the fact that the Aa text contains a great many interpolations and is otherwise tampered with. Before int. 5: 17 sine fine, Aa adds semper et, and instead of int. 6: 22 sciret this MS. has sentiret et sciret. Furthermore, it has a great number of individual errors, e.g. the omission of int. 2: 7 superbia and of int. 6: 27 magis. Interpolations seem to be typical of Ma, too. In int. 4: 10 Ma has timendus sum instead of timendum est, after int. 5: 2 hominibus the words et seruis dei were added; for int. 6: 2 sunt it has procedunt, for int. lo: 18 odio it has odi (cf. Ib j v (a) All the n readings from section 1 appear in the MS. Ib. This MS. has the arrangement of the int. 13 text (including the responsiones) typical of the y group and, furthermore, it has all the other y errors given in section 3. Since Ib does not have any of the errors listed for X w x on p. 52 f., I checked the errors which occur in n, and found that many of the n errors appear in Ib as well. In the follow- ing examples the y hyparchetype had, in all probability, the correct reading as X w x proved to have a correct text: int. 8: 16 ingressurus for ingressus, int. 13: 61 nisi for nec, int. 15: 25 differre for differt, and int. 16: 48 audito for audire. Errors in n not found in Ib exclude the possibility that Ib was copied from n, e.g. the eorum and of int. 14: 17 et ... labore. Other n omission of int. 12: 40 et quia errors are comparatively many, but I did not find more than three cases, in which n has a correct text whereas Ib has an error. All three cases are from int. 13, § 15 datur (cf. the preceding datur) instead of dator, ,§ 44 temporis for temporibus, and § 57 cadit for cadat. These cases are not strong enough to rule out Ib as the original of n, and such a relationship between the two MSS. is most convincingly suggested by int. 15: 49 purganda, instead of which Ib has purgenda. The n copyist first wrote purganda, but he changed this into purgenda.52 It is hard to believe that he made this alteration because he consideredpurgenda better than purganda, it is, of course, more probable that he took it from his original, which was apparently P. In view of this diligence on the n copyist's part, the cases mentioned above of correct n readings against Ib errors were more probably caused by unintentional diver- gencies from Ib than by deliberate emendations. It may be added that the theory that P was the original of n is corroborated by the fact that both MSS. contain 52 The reading in X w is here purgandi, while x has purgandam.

 63 errors quoted above, and these facts seem to indicate that j belongs to the X w x branch of the y tradition, perhaps an early stage of it. Many individual errors, however, as weil as intentional textual modifications, render this MS. a fairly bad representative of the y hyparchetype. It has, for instance, "tem instead of Tu in int. i: 4-7 (five cases), graciam for int. 6: i i faciem, it adds nimis before int. 13: 5 gratulantur (cf. the preceding nimis indurantur), and ibid. § 17 it has, instead of redderet, the reading reddere debet. An example of modified word order in j was given above on p. 45 (int. 2: 5). (c) A considerably more reliable picture of the y hyparchetype is furnished by MS. v. This MS. has, like Ib, all the n readings from section i (though it is illegible in int. 16: 14 aliqua), and it has all the y errors as weil, included the y arrangement of int. 13. Although v has one of the Ib n errors listed above in this section, viz. int. 8: 16 ingressurus instead of ingressus, I am more inclined to range v in the X w x branch of the y text, for it has the X w x (j) reading aliquo- modo for int. 4: 3 aut quomodo and, instead of int. 6: i i electi, it has dilecti, also an X w x (j) reading. With regard to these similarities between v and X w x (j), one can explain int. 8: 16 ingressurus either as an error independently made in each of Ib (n) and v, or as the reading of the y hyparchetype, corrected in X w x j. The former theory is confirmed by some other cases, not discussed above, in which v has the same error as X w x, or some of these MSS.: in Prol.: 7 there is in X w x v an in added before corpore, and in the case of int. 13: 27 w x v have the reading formidandus instead of formidandum. Individual errors in v exclude the possibility that it was used as the original of any other MS. examined, e.g. the omission of int. 4: 9 misericordia ... appropinquat and the read- ing per instead of the correct perfeccione in int. 6: 12. Finally, one case may be discussed which is of some interest as regards the evaluation of the y MSS. examined. In int. 13: 8, the words ad solacium hominis creata sunt are omitted from X. Instead of solacium, P n have the reading con- solacium, whereas w x j v have consolacionem. This latter reading is very easily explained as a `Verschlimmbesserung' of consolacium; on the other hand I did not find any parallel to consolacium, so any intentional alteration of consolacionem into consolacium is out of the question. An assumed consolacionem in the -y hypar- chetype might have been unintentionally changed into consolacium, but the simi- larity between solacium and consolacium would make it more probable that con- solacium did in fact occur in the y hyparchetype, which clearly had a word starting with con-. The copyist who wrote the y hyparchetype may have written con- solacionem first, and later corrected it after checking with his original; alternatively, he may have observed his mistake before the whole word was written. The deletion of con-, if it really was carried out, passed down unnoticed in both branches of the tradition, but in Ib n no attempt was made to emend the erroneous con- solacium. The analysis thus supports the results of the earlier Ib examination in so far as this MS. must be assumed to reflect the y hyparchetype better than other MSS. of the group.

Summary The eleven best MSS. from my edition of Rev. VII (BFKOPYmneir0a) and thirteen other MSS. (c i j v Aa Ma pa Wa Ab Db Eb Fb Ib) , not taken into consideration in that edition, were examined and found to fall into two main branches, called f and 7t. The former branch contains two different groups, and p,. The pl group is formed by P and Y, though the former MS. is contaminated with n (y) readings; in Rev. VII it was found to represent the a group. The p, group includes B 0 c m Oa Ab, and Ab was apparently the original of m. Within 56 As regards rev. 12: 6 infigit, however, there is an omission (part of the sentence) in Eb. 5 — 694476 Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 66 the n branch there are also sub-groups. One of these is y =j n v Ib; in all probability n was copied from Ib. Another sub-group, a, is formed by F K q pa Wa Db Eb Fb; the text of the last of these MSS., Fb, is evidently dependent upon q. MSS. i r also belong to the n branch. Common errors indicate a connexion between these two MSS.; as distinct from its text of Rev. V, r gives the p, text of Rev. VII. Two further MSS., Aa and ma, belong to the rt family, though the interpolations in their texts render their place within the tradition uncertain.  Principles of Edition Manuscripts Used for the Edition. The Apparatus Since the tradition of the Liber questionum consists of two branches, the archetype is in principle reconstructed by means of a comparison between these two branches, designated p and 7r. When the two branches present the same reading, there is of course no question about the archetypal reading. When they present different readings—these cases are, however, comparatively few—the choice between them is in some places problematic, in so far as contextual and linguistic arguments cannot be used to rule out either reading as incorrect, e.g. int. 7, 44 f. omnia temporalia bona p bona temporalia omnia r Gh. In the present edition the p reading is in such cases preferred to r, since the investigation of the MSS. carried out in the preceding chapter indicates that the r text, as opposed to the text of p, was submitted to a certain normalization; cf. in particular pp. 43, 46. The branches of the tradition were evaluated in the same way in the Rev. VII investigation; cf. Bergh 94. For the reconstruction of the different hyparchetypes the MSS. are used in the edition as follows. A comparison between p, and p, makes it possible to establish the text of the p hyparchetype: the consensus of P and Y is then taken to correspond to the pi reading (see p. 51 f.), whereas B 0 Ab are used for the reconstruction of the 132 reading. The MS. Ab, not used in the edition of Rev. VII, replaces m (cf. p. 59); as regards Oa, cf. p. 51. For the establishment of the n text I use r, y and a. Though P is to be considered a very good representative of the y recension (cf. p. 61 ff.), I found it nevertheless necessary to adopt one further y MS., representing the other branch of the y tradition, in order to make a distinc- tion in the apparatus between errors in the y hyparchetype and individual P errors. For this purpose I chose v (cf. p. 63), and the consensus of P and v is regarded to be identical with the reading of the y hyparchetype, although there is some uncertainty in the case of int. 8: 16 ingressus, discussed above on pp. 61 and 63.1 In the present edition the y text is thus reconstructed by means of two MSS., neither of which was used in the Rev. VII edition. Finally, the a. text is in the edition represented by F K q; as regards Gh, which is also to be ranged in the a family (cf. p. 53), see below. The critical apparatus informs of the sources used and of readings in these, diverging from the printed text. It also includes references and parallels, but only 1 The v reading additurus for int. 9: 20 additus indicates that the Ib v reading ingressurus in int. 8: 16 was not necessarily handed down in both MSS. 
 
 
 
 
 68 if they are considered to justify the edited text or elucidate a certain reading. Other references have been brought together in a separate apparatus. Numbers without paragraph sign indicate line of the chapter. The hyparchetype symbols are used only if the hyparchetype can be assumed to have had the reading in question. If one reading is said to be in p, and another in 0, for instance, this implies both that 0 has a reading diverging from the other p, MSS., and that the latter MSS. are considered to reflect the p, hyparchetype. The text of the Gh edition is continuously accounted for in the apparatus (cf. Bergh 98); as regards the Gh headings of the reuelaciones, see below, p. 72. The letters in the margin refer to the paragraphs into which the Gh text is divided. The Headings The beginning of Rev. V is differently arranged in different sources; the majority, however, of the MSS.,2 including P Y B Ab Ib v K q, present the same ar- rangement, which was undoubtedly characteristic of the archetype as well, since it is represented by nearly all tradition branches. In these MSS. the book is intro- duced by the following text, generally preceded by some incipit: Liber iste questio- num diuiditur per interrogaciones et subdiuiditur per questiones. Habet enim XVI interrogaciones et in qualibet earum iudex christus interrogatur de certis questionibus ad quas ipse respondet distincte et mirabiliter ita quod quelibet interrogacio continet certum numerum questionum. Postea vero sequuntur earum soluciones et responsiones prout in processu libri lacius continetur. This text, here designated A, is followed by a list of contents which consists of headings corresponding to those occurring later in the text,3 and after this the prologue is written, introduced by some incipit. After a new incipit the text then starts with Vidi thronum etc. ( = int. i: i in the present edition). In r and F the text A is lacking, as is the list of contents: in these MSS. the book is introduced by the prologue, then follows Vidi thronum etc. (preceded by an incipit in F). The same beginning is typical of Rev. V in 0 (incipits before the prologue and before Vidi thronum etc.). The MS. 0 possesses, unlike r and F, a formal list of contents containing references to all the books, placed at the end of the MS. (fol. I8orb ff.); in the list of Rev. V (fol. 186rb ff.) the text A is lacking. Finally, the arrangement in Gh implies that the prologue (with an incipit) is followed by the text A. There is no list of contents in Gh, and pre- ceded by an incipit, Vidi thronum etc. follows the text A. The placing of the text A in the archetype, i.e. before the list of contents, gives reason to believe that it was written in connexion with the list of contents, intended to introduce this list and to inform of the chief characteristic of Rev. V, viz. the interrogaciones; this seems to explain why the text A does not mention the inserted reuelaciones, the 2 From now on the MS. material will be represented only by the eleven MSS. pointed out in the previous section of this chapter. 3 In the K list, the heading of rev. 2 is lacking. 
 
 
 
 
 69 headings of which are, in fact, included in the list. This list of contents was ob- viously considered as dispensable by the editors of r, F and Gh, but while the text A was omitted from r and F—most probably because it was taken to be closely connected with the list of contents—it was retained in Gh. As opposed to the headings included in the list, the text A appears only once in the book (such as the book was edited in the archetype), and it would seem that this fact led the Gh editors to drop the list of contents but keep the text A. Placed after Alphonso's prologue in Gh it gives, however, a very odd impression, since it furnishes less information than the prologue without adding anything essentially new. Although the list of contents was present in the archetype, I did not find it necessary to include it in the edition, since the same material appears in the form of headings later in the book (as regards the headings of the reuelaciones, however, see below, p. 7o ff.). Consequently the text A was also excluded from the edition. In order to give a survey of the contents of Rev. V—and most probably the list of contents in the archetype was due to the same redactional intention—I pre- ferred to make a new table of contents for this edition (p. 95); the title on p. 93 was also made by me, without any particular regard to the rubrics adopted in the different MSS. On the other hand Alphonso's prologue is edited, though in small print (cf. Bergh 97, 113). It is introduced by a heading which is identical with the incipit of the prologue occurring in the f32 MSS., Incipit prologus quinti libri celestium reuelacionum qui dicitur liber questionum. The same incipit is given by Y, but this MS. has the reading celestis instead of celestium; cf. above, p. 5o. No incipit is th en given in the edition to introduce the text after the prologue, nor does the edition contain any of the variously formed explicits of the MSS., either after the prologue or at the end of the book (cf. Bergh 97). In the literature the interrogaciones of Rev. V are generally referred to by indi- cation of the questio or the responsio which includes the passage under discussion, e.g. "interrogacio 16, responsio 3" (Kraft 92, footnote 2). In the present edition each interrogacio has been divided into comparatively small, numbered paragraphs, but for the identification of references made according to the earlier system, I found it necessary to denote by headings the different questiones and responsiones. Most f3 MSS. have the type of heading exemplified by Interrogacio quarta continet quinque questiones; the headings introducing the different questiones are then either written in the margin or omitted. In the p MSS. the responsiones section is introduced by a phrase of the type Responsio christi iudicis ad primam questionem, and for the following responsiones these MSS. either omit the heading or have it written in the margin; in the latter case the heading is of the type Responsio secunda etc. The majority of the n MSS. have, as compared to the p headings mentioned, Interro- gacio quarta as chief heading, then Questio prima, Questio secunda etc., and for the responsiones they have Responsio questionis prime etc., or Responsiones. Questio prima etc. Since the p hyparchetype possibly lacked headings to the different questiones and responsiones, I found it necessary to depart from the general principle of edition, which favours 3, in order to carry out the intention of denoting by head- 
 
 
 
 
 70 ings all questiones and responsiones.4 In that situation I preferred to adopt the headings of the Gh edition, although with some minor divergencies. Instead of the figures XII, XIII, XIIII, used in Gh of the headings of int. 12-14, I write duo- decima, terciadecima and quartadecima in accordance with the other headings in Gh. All interrogaciones are thus numbered by ordinals written in letters, and the choice of ordinals is consistent with the designations duodecima, terciadecima, quintadecima and sextadecima, used in Gh and several MSS. of rev. 12-13 and int. 15-16 respectively.5 Another discrepancy from Gh concerns the placing of the heading Interrogacio prima: in Gh, as in the majority of the MSS., the heading of int. i is placed immediately before int. i: 3 according to my edition; my placing of it, supported by P and Y, facilitates references to the text Vidi thronum etc. and is thus justified by practical reasons. A similar case is int. 9: i. The headings Interrogacio nona and Questio prima are in the present edition placed as in F K Gh, but in the archetype the heading of int. 9 seems to have been placed after § i dicens, since that is the placing typical of p r y q. The headings of the differ- ent questiones and responsiones have been taken over from the Gh edition without any changes. The difference in word order between the headings of the responsiones in the two first interrogaciones and all the following responsiones thus corresponds to Gh, as does the difference in wording between the heading of int. i: 8 and other comparable headings (Responsio Christi ad primam questionem as compared to Responsio prime questionis or Responsio questionis prime). A special problem is raised by the headings of the 13 inserted reuelaciones. In all headings there are discrepancies between p and n, but p n also display manifest similarities, of a kind which makes it necessary to assume a dependence; a clear 4 It seems reasonable to assume that Birgitta's own original lacked headings, at least those introducing the different questiones and responsiones; the word item may have functioned as an head- ing. It is besides very probable that the word item was used already in Birgitta's OS original: in a letter to her son Birger, written in OS (see BU 5, 140 f.), she USCS item five times. 5 Instead of terciadecima and quartadecima in the headings of int. 13-14, it would have been pos- sible to adopt the type of ordinal exemplified by A&P 164 decimaquinta (sc. jndiccione) or, p. 73, decima septima (sc. die; i.e. with the ordinal written in two words). In A&P this numbering is used by the side of the type exemplified by p. 40 quartadecima (sc. die); cf. Thes. 5: 1, 172, 6o ff. On the other hand that sort of ordinal is not to be found either in the Revelations or in A&P, which in Habel-Gröbers dictionary is represented by decimononus and decimoquintus. Certainly there are some cases of decimo- in A&P, but only with the ablative form of articulus or dies (in the masculine gender), e.g. p. 215 decimoquinto articulo and p. 59 decimooctauo die (similarly Nic. proc. 66 decimo- septimo, se. anno). However, the parallels from A&P just mentioned make it necessary to look upon decimo- in these cases as a mere ablative form; cf. cases like p. 248 decimo sexto articulo and p. 356 decimo octauo articulo, i.e. in both cases the ordinal is written in two words (similarly Nic. proc. 316 decimo quinto articulo). Of course this does not necessarily imply that decimononus and decimo- quintus listed by Habel-Gröbel are due to a false interpretation of the ablative masc. sing. form of decimusnonus and decimusquintus, but this particular form of ordinal is not listed in any other Me- dieval Latin dictionary; the very late instance decimoctavus (1569), listed in P1, is explicitly stated by the lexicographer to be composed of decem and octavus, thus representing yet another kind of ordinal. 
 
 
 
 
 71 example of that is given by rev. 4:R, quoted below. On the other hand the 13 headings, in differing from n, belong to the same textual recension as is repre- sented by the headings included in the list of contents, here designated list headings, to distinguish them from the text headings. The connexion between the list head- ings in p rc and the [3 text headings may be illustrated by the following examples. As compared to rev. ER Reuelacio prima in libro questionum facta domine brigide a virgine maria in qua informat eam virgo maria de ... in the sources mentioned, the n text heading begins with the words Loquitur virgo maria beate birgitte informando eam de ...; as to the respective assessment of domine and beate cf. above, p. 21 with footnote 21. In rev. 3:R the n text heading has doctrinam in comparison with quandam doctrinam in the two other sources, a peccando for a peccato, and the words per eius doctrinam are lacking. Instead of rev. 4:R moralizans dicta membra spiri- tualiter comparando ea virtutibus et pronunciat eandem virginem dignissimam esse reginali corona in the p rc list heading and the p text heading, the n text heading has moralizans ea spiritualiter et pronuncians eam dignissimam reginali corona. For rev. 12:R subiungit, the latter source has the reading submittit. The context here seems to require a verb with the meaning `add', and subiungere is in the Revelations often used in this sense (as in Classical Latin), e.g. 141: 34, II 13 E, IV 7 K, 126 E, VI 17 B, 32 D, VII 31: 11; the same usage in Ep. Sol. 3 E and in A&P 401, 629. I did not find any instance of submittere in this function, but on the other hand that verb is to be exemplified in the Revelations in its normal functions, e.g. VII 15: 23 Habebat ... ventrem submissum coherentem dorso. When comparing the three texts in question, i.e. the list headings, the 3 text headings and the n text headings, it seems very probable that the n text headings were modelled on the list headings, whereas it does not appear very likely that the n text headings were the source of any of the other texts: an indication of this is e.g. rev. 4:R, quoted above, where the words comparando ea virtutibus are lacking in the n text heading. The possibility that the p text headings were used as the source of the other texts is less probable in view of some divergencies in the former from the latter. In rev. 3:R, for instance, the p text heading has falso interfectore as compared to falso medico interfectore in the other texts, and in the text heading of rev. 12, p lacks ipse (line 2) and isto (line 5). The third possibility, viz. that the list headings were, or represent, the source of the two other texts, is the most plausible of all: only in the case of rev. 13:R a list heading diverges from a p n text heading, inasmuch as it includes the words Et sequitur exposicio ... in figura, lacking in the f n text heading, from which they, however, may very well have been intentionally omit- ted. The text of the headings of the reuelaciones thus seems to have its best repre- sentative in the list of contents,6 and in view of this I found it logical to introduce in the edition each reuelacio by the corresponding list heading. With regard to the similarities between the list headings and the p text headings, the method implies that the text of the latter is at the same time on the whole well accounted for in the , The archetype may have lacked text headings. 
 
 
 
 
 72 edition, but the reader will get a rather good picture of the n text headings as well, since the headings of Gh, which lacks the list of contents (cf. above), are printed separately in the apparatus in consistence with the prinicple, put forward above on p. 68, of giving running information of the Gh text. Orthography In orthographical matters the edition follows the principles presented in the edition of Rev. VII; see Bergh 98 f. The spelling has thus been normalized to accord with the usage generally observed in the MSS., and orthographical diver- gencies in the sources are very seldom listed in the apparatus. Among the diffe- rences from the Classical Latin spelling, not accounted for in the Glossary (pp. 176-179), one may call attention to the spelling e for original ae and oe (cf. Norberg 19); furthermore, i and y are very often confused, e.g. dyabolus, ydiota, ymago, ymmo and, conversely, Apocrifi, Egiptus, misterium (Norberg 52). The spelling is in the MSS. rather inconsistent as to the letter h, which is in some words placed erroneously, in others wrongly added or omitted. Examples in the edition are phitonissa; heremus, häs, and the striking spelling trahiciatur in int. i i: 22; calibs, edus (Norberg 22, 51 with lit.). As regards michi, nichil, cf. Norberg 52 with lit., Svennung, Från senantik och medeltid. II, p. 8. For u and v, the spelling v is adopted initially, otherwise u is used. An epenthetical p is to be observed in words such as dampnum, verumptamen (cf. Norberg 52, Svennung op. cit. 13). Conso- nants are in some words doubled or single-written, as compared to the Classical Latin spelling, e.g. illico, tollerare; consumare, solercia (Norberg 52). The prefixes ad-, con-, in-, ob-, sub- generally appear assimilated; no assimilation occurs, however, in obfuscare and obprobrium. Before a vowel the spelling ti is retained in the combinations sti and xti, otherwise ci, e.g. eciam (Norberg 22, 5i). Birgitta's name is spelled Birgitta throughout; other spellings in the MSS. are brigitta and brigida (cf. Undhagen 171). All the headings have been italicized in order to indicate their secondary character within the text (cf. above, p. 69 ff.). In the use of capitals and in punctuation no regard is paid to the MSS. Other Remarks After int. 16: 37 there is in Gh added a declaracio concerning Magister Mathias, and with some divergencies the same text appears in F as well, but it is written in the margin by a later hand (fol. 2o2r). Arguing against Schfick, Kraft holds (p. 92, footnote 2) that this text was written in Vadstena shortly before the printing of the Gh edition in 1492 (cf. Westman 46, footnote 6), and in Kraft's view it was not included in the collection of Birgittine texts which Prior Petrus deposited at Alvastra in 138o (cf. Bergh 18 with lit.), texts which were incorporated into post- Alphonsine editions of the Revelations, to a great extent in the form of addiciones or declaraciones to different chapters (cf. above, p. 46, footnote 23). Kraft's argu- ment in defense of his opinion is to be found on p. 76, where he says that the text 
 
 
 
 
 73 in question appears at the earliest as a marginal supplement in a younger MS. (cf. Kraft 71, footnote i); in all probability MS. F is intended. Kraft's dating of the text was criticized by Hollman (p. 35), whose objection was that it exists already in F, i.e. a fact mentioned by Kraft. However, Hollman does not say that it was added by a later hand in the MS., thus giving a somewhat misleading impression of the situation. On the other hand, it is in Hollman's opinion an open question whether the text was really included in Petrus' collections, and obviously it never was. A very good proof of that is the fact that it is lacking in the redaction of the additional material made in Vadstena about, or some decade before, the middle of the i 5th century, a redaction which is known from the Cod. Holm. A 22, foll. 21-54; see Hollman 40 ff., 56. Furthermore, a control was made in the i5th century Cod. 392o in the National Library of Vienna ( = Q), which contains the addiciones of Rev. VII (see Bergh 62, 7o ff.), but the text is lacking in that MS. as well. Contrary to what is implicitly suggested by Hollman on p. 36, footnote 1, no addiciones or declaraciones were thus at all incorporated in any hand-written edition of Rev. V. The presence of a Gh text in F2 is not exceptional. To the similarities between Gh and Fl, discussed above on p. 53, the following between Gh and F2 may be listed: int. 13: 44 is in both sources preceded by the words Nota exemplum de altro fructuoso et infructuoso, and in int. 12: 13 there is another F2 reading which corresponds to the text in Gh (see app. crit.). In the edition the declaracio after int. 16: 37 is given in the apparatus, as is the case of the other Gh F2 readings. 
 
 
 
 
 Textual Criticism The archetype of the Liber questionum tradition, such as it is reconstructed according to the principles put forward in the preceding chapter, exhibits in some places readings which were probably not present in the primary codex of the Latin tradition; consequently, the archetype and this primary codex would not have been identical.1 In this concluding chapter some passages will be discussed, where the reading of the reconstructed archetype for different reasons gave rise to sus- picions regarding its authenticity; in ten of the fourteen passages examined the archetype was deemed to diverge from the original wording. rev. 3: 2 Medicus igitur sciens infirmitatem infirmi, si dat ei medicamentum, quo mors pr ouenit (v F K Gh peruenit p r Ib q), arguitur tamquam interfector et non est verus medicus. Obviously the archetype had the reading peruenit, given by the p MSS. and sufficiently supported by representatives of the n branch. The context seems to require a verb with the meaning 'come about', `follow', and peruenire is not to be found in the Revelations (nor in Classical Latin texts) in that function. Further- more, the instances of peruenire taken from the Revelations are syntactically very homogeneous, in so far as the verb is constructed with ad in nearly all cases, viz. int. 6: 15, int. i o: 4, rev. 6: 10 (two cases), int. 16: 16, rev. 12: 8; Rev. I 2: 12, 29: 5-6, IV 2 E, 4 C, 7 H, 7o B, Decl. VI 15, Rev. VI 35 B, 39 M, Add. VI 41, Rev. VI 57 C (two cases), 62 A, 67 A, VII 19: 17, Ex. 68: 3, SA 8: 16, 16: 2.9. The material includes only two exceptions, but the difference concerns the choice of preposition: in SA 14: 14 and 20: 14 peruenire is constructed with in, not with ad.2 The verb prouenire is used in the Revelations with meanings known from Classical Latin, including 'come about', `follow', assumed above for our case rev. 3: 2. In the majority of the cases there is a preposition or an adverb, denoting origin or cause (a: SA 5: 3, ex: int. 13: 35, int. 16: 35, Rev. VI 9 D, 29:R, 3o E, 43 B, VII 12: 46, SA 4: 16, inde: SA lo: 9, 14: 13, propter: int. 13: 36), but there are also cases without any expression of that kind (int. 14: 24-25.32, Rev. II 19 C, III 5 A). In our case, quo seems to exemplify the abl. causae, and even though the material 1 The investigation into the Rev. VII tradition gave the same result; cf. Bergh zoo if. is thus typical of peruenire that it is combined with an expression of direction, and a case like ire potuit, illuc tercia die penthecostes perueniens is consistent A&P 1 33 ad monasterium Wastenam with that. 2 It 
 
 
 
 
 75 does not contain any certain example of prouenire combined with an ablative (cf. however int. 14: 12, discussed below p. 78 f.), one sannot consider such a con- struction particularly striking in view of the other constructions exemplified. The archetypal error peruenit in rev. 3: 2, corrected in v F K Gh and in the present edition, has parallels both in manuscript sources and printed editions. In Rev. VII 12: 46, MS. x has peruenire instead of the correct prouenire, and the Gh edition has in Rev. VI 29:R the readingperueniunt for proueniunt. A comparable case is to be found in DSS III 23, where the reading proueniamus in two MSS. was by the editor wrongly preferred to perueniamus in the two other sources (donec ad patriam celestem perueniamus; cf. U. Westerbergh in ALMA 35, 1967, 73).3 Cf. also Undhagen 151. rev. 3: 16 Ergo, quia de illo genere sunt illi, quorum sanguis et cor querit altes, et eo plus sitit eorum voluntas, quo plus bibit, ideo amicus eorum non tribuat eis occasionem excedendi; quia incendere (Ab F intendere 0 incedere pi Bry q [ex incendere corr. q] excedere K Gh) cupiunt sed (si appetitum extinguere non valent. Thes. 8: i, 853, 65 ff. and 854, 71; Be 1-2), usually in combination with an adverb or a pre- positional expression (rev. 3: 11, Rev. I 6: 4, 15: 5, 23: 2, 40: 5, 41: 8, II 15 D. E, III 2 A, VI 23 B, VII 2: 3, Ex. 17: 4, Reg. Salt'. 1 i B; cf. A&P 74, 138, 213, 615, DSS VI 256), it does not seem possible to retain it as the authentic reading. The original had in all probability incendere:4 the linea nasalis was dropped in the archetype (cf. Thes. 7: i, 865, 57 f.). The meaning of the quia clause is then "for they are eager to set fire to their desire but are not capable of slaking it", the word appetitum being the object of both incendere and extinguere. As regards incendere followed by libidinem, cupiditatem and similar objects, see Thes. 7: i, 869, 39 if. As regards incendere in Ab F and intendere in 0, it seems impossible to ascertain whether these readings are due to intentional corrections,6 while excedere in K Gh probably is of that kind.? rev. 4: 19 Huius itaque ventris (Ab r2 F K Gh venter f3 1.1 y q), idest fidei tue, parietes fuerunt quasi aurum fulgentissimum It is reasonable to assume that the archetype had the reading huius venter, which was handed down to all branches of the tradition. The authentic reading, ventris, being the genitive form of hic venter and func- however, must be huius tioning as the attribute of parietes. In the context Virgin Mary's venter is used as a simile of her faith, which is explicitly said by idest fidei tue but already suggested quia constancia consciencie tue et fidei... in § 18 V enter tuus fuit mundissimus Thus the reading ventris, into which venter was corrected in some sources, is adopted in the present edition. int. 11: 17 Sed nec ipsi prophete viderunt me, sicut sum in mee (y K in mea p r a Gh nunc P) deitatis (deitas P deitate Y) natura (uera pl), The reading in mea seems to have been in the archetype, but since I cannot find any arguments strong enough against the natural assumption that the possessive pronoun here refers to deitatis and not to natura, I take mea to be an error for mee, thus adopting the emendation made in y K. With regard to these parallels it could be maintained that the word order in natura deitatis mee would in our case have been more consistent with the normal usage, and perhaps this difference was a contributory cause of the archetypal error mea. int. 12: 29 Vt ergo homo saluaretur, iustum et dignum fuit, vt aliquis talis veniret, qui et (eum F Gh) r e dim er e t, qui et obedienciam perfectam haberet et innocenciam et in quo, qui vellent, possent ostendere caritatem et, qui vellent, maliciam. In F and Gh the reading et redimeret was changed into eum redimeret, with eum referring to homo in the beginning of the sentence. Most probably this alteration was made because redimeret was regarded as requiring an accusative object. Is it then possible to retain the reading of the archetype, thus considering an absolute function of redimere correct in this context? As a matter of fact, such a usage can be exemplified in some OT places of the Vulgate, e.g. Levit. 27, 27 Quod si im- mundum est animal, r e dirnet qui obtulit iuxta aestimationem tuam, Ps. 7, 2 f. salvum me fac ex omnibus persequentibus me et libera me, ne quando rapiat ut leo animam meam, dum non est qui r e dimat neque qui salvum faciat, Ps. 48, 8 Frater non r edimit, r edimet homo, Is. 5o, 2 numquid abbreviata et parvula facta est manus mea, ut non gossim r edimer e?, Lam. 4, 8 Servi dominati sunt nostri; non fuit qui r edimer et de manu eorum; the second and the last of these cases recall our case int. 12: 29. The same usage appears in the Revelations as weil, I 59: 35 0 amici mei, sic tenere diligo oues istas, quod, si possibile esset adhuc mori propter quamlibet ouem speciali morte, qualem in cruce semel pro omnibus passus sum, magis r e dim er em quam eis carerem; with regard to the F Gh reading eum in int. 12: 29 is it not sur- prising to see that the same sources (and K) add, in Rev. 159: 35, eas before redi- merem. Another medieval instance of the absolute use of redimere is furnished by the i oth tristich of the well-known hymn Dies irae, dies illa (attributed to Thomas de Celano, i3th century), Quaerens me viasti lassus, redemisti crucem passus.8 Considering the material presented here, it would appear necessary to accept the archetypal reading et redimeret as authentic. The meaning of et is then = eciam as, for instance, in int. 13: 52; its function is thus different from the corresponding et ... et in the following relative clause. rev. 8: 7 Nempe celestia non intelligo nisi ex auditu, et vtrum credendum sit scriptu- ris, nescio. Tamen (K Gh cum p 7c), si habere possem voluntatem meam, hoc reci- perem pro celesti regno. If the archetypal reading cum reciperem is really correct, then it seems most natural to take the clause to exemplify the adversative use of cum (cf. Hofmann- Szantyr 619). However, since it was not possible to find any parallels of cum used H. A. Daniel, Thesaurus hymnologicus. II. Leipzig 1844, p. 104. 
 
 
 
 
 78 in this way in the Revelations, I found it reasonable to suspect its authenticity in this passage. Furthermore, the reading with cum gives a rather complicated syntax, and with regard to the fact that H/ISS. (and printed editions) from time to time exhibit cum erroneously for tamen (see e.g. int. 9, 85),9 it is necessary to ask whether the K Gh reading tamen does not correspond to the original wording. With some hesitation I adopted that reading in the edition. Parallels to tamen introducing a new sentence are given by Rev. 151: 15 (tamen), 18: 4 and 33: 4 (sed tamen), int. 15: 38 (et tamen). The parallel text in Rev. VI 19 (cf. above, p. 18 footnote 17) is differently formed in this passage, so a comparison between the two texts does not throw light on the problem. int. 14: 12 Nam aut erit (sc. the sufferings of the animals) eis ad celeriorem vite finem aut ad minorem miserie laborem et nature fortis consumpcionem aut propter mutacio- nem temporum aut ex incuria hominis labore pr oueniente (Ab2 r y KGh proue- nientem p, Abf F q preuenientem B 0). Considering the distribution of the different readings in the tradition, the archetype is most likely to have had prouenientem. If this is the correct reading, then it must be an adjunct of consumpcionem and constructed with the two co- ordinated members aut propter ... and aut ex ..., and as was demonstrated above on p. 74, the verb prouenire can certainly be combined with both propter and ex. This may have been the original reading, and the meaning would then be "the consumption of their nature which arises either because of the change of times or from ..." Certain complications are, however, involved in this interpretation. One concerns the presence of the other aut in the same sentence: it would be reasonable at least to suspect that they all stand on the same level in the sense of "either or ... or ... or", but in that case prouenientem cannot refer to consumpcionem. A particular problem is raised by the words incuria hominis labore, not translated above. It is not easy to find any good explanation of the ablative form labore, if ex incuria is taken to be governed by prouenientem. The same complications are inherent in the alternative given by B 0, viz. the reading preuenientem; if we disregard these complications, the verb preuenire would be theoretically possible in the context, if it had the meaning luppen in advance'. The verb preuenire does not, however, seem to have been used in the Revelations with that meaning, whereas the transitive function with the meaning `forestall', `anticipate' is found e.g. in int. 15: 38 f. and Rev. IV 74 A (three times). The reading proueniente, functioning as an adjunct of incuria, makes it possible to give an acceptable explanation of labore. In this alternative, which was adopted in the edition, I take labore to have the same function as quo in the case of rev. 3: 2 quo mors prouenit, discussed above on p. 74 f. The phrase ex incuria hominis labore proueniente then means something like "because of the carelessness which follows 9 Conversely, tamen sometimes stands for cum, e.g. Rev. VI 3o: R (Gh). Cf. above, p. 25, foot- note 31 (tamen for tu), and below, p. 84 (rev. i i: lo). 
 
 
 
 
 79 when they (i.e. the animals) are working for man", if hominis is governed by labore, or "because of man's carelessness, resulting from their work", if it is gov- erned by incuria. Certainly the word incuria is usually combined with a genitive (e.g. int. 6: 9 and int. 15: 47) but, on the other hand, such a construction is not at all necessary, as is evident from a case like D SS VI 3o f. ut bene complicent (sc. clerici) et custodiant libros, ne destruantur propter in c ur i am. In my opinion, hominis is governed by labore. The expression hominis labor, "work in man's service", may seem to imply a less usual function of the genitive, but apparently the same kind of genitive occurs in the following passage, taken from Iohannes de Plano Carpini, Ystoria Mongalorum (ed. Wyngaert), p. 92 Sed aliqui tam malos dominos habent quod nichil eis dimittunt, nec habent tempus pre multitudine operum dominorum ut sibi aliquid operentur. int. 14: 18 Animalia eciam ideo pariunt cum dolore, vt excessum temperet amaritudo et par ticipes (p n [ex particeps corr. F] partict:pia K Gh) sint hominis in labore et dolore. The archetypal reading animalia participes implies a striking formal in- congruity, which was obviously not accepted by the editors of K and Gh. With the use of a parallel from another passage in the Revelations it seems, however, ne- cessary to assume that the archetype has really preserved the original reading. In Rev. VII 12: 28 the text runs De indumentis vestris consulo, quod numquam plus insimul quam tria paria in possessione habeatis, et omnes (omnia C k K q Gh), que (qui I L w x P) superfuerint, statim ipsi Deo tribuatis. In all probability the words omnes que (which were present in the archetype) refer to an understood vestes, synonymous with indumenta (cf. the variation indumenta vestes in Rev. VII 7: i but in some sources among which K and Gh are to be noted, omnes was taken to be an error for omnia (sc. indumenta); the passage was discussed by Bergh 101.10 In our case the word participes most probably refers to an understood bestie. lo The case of Rev. VII 12: 28 was commented upon by A. Önnerfors in Mittellateinisches Jahr- buch 5, 1968, 292. Önnerfors finds the theory of an understood vestes less convincing and proposes omnes qui, which is the reading of I L w x P, and Önnerfors holds that the passage may exemplify indumentum in the masculine gender. To support this idea he refers to a case from the 4th century, listed in Thes. 7: i, 1259, 73, viz. Serm. Arian. fragm., PL 13, 636B Sicut enim indumentum, quod schon einen spätlateinischen uteris, alter confecit vel texuit, et eum indueris. In saying "gibt es Beleg", Önnerfors suggests that there is a Medieval Latin use of masc. indumentum with roots in a Late Latin usage. However, no other case of masc. indumentum is to be found in the whole Thes. material, nor in Medieval Latin dictionaries, and it must be considered surprising that neither the Thes. editor (7: i, 1259, 52), nor Önnerfors takes eum in the case mentioned to exemplify eum erroneously used instead of id. In Chronicon Salernitanum (ed. U. Westerbergh), p. 31, 5 and 7, there is an eum referring to indumentum, but with good reasons the editor takes this eum to be of the neuter gender (p. 264). For Late Latin examples of eum = id, see Thes. 7: 2, 457, 75 if. and Hofmann-Szantyr 188 with lit. It may be added that the same error occurs in the Revelations, too, SA 6: 2 creauit hominem, tribuens ei liberum arbitrium, vt per eum in bono perseueranter staret, and I think nobody will use this case to support a theory of masculine arbitrium in the Revelations. Thes. 2, 1935, 38 if.), whereas animal seems to denote animal in a more general sense, i.e. animal as distinguished from man, this cannot be said to be any serious obstacle to the assumption made above: the animals, of which Jesus Christ is speaking to Birgitta in int. 14: 18, naturally include the wild beasts as weil. Besides, looking into other Medieval Latin texts it appears that bestia is by no means restricted to denoting `wild animal', and an illustrative example of that is to be found in A&P 428 in silua Fossignana, ubi idem testis habebat pluresbestias bouinas (cf. MW and SG1 s.v.); in Catalan Medieval Latin the word bestia actually seems to have been used about domesticated animals in particular (see CG1 s.v.). int. 14: 25 Propterea, quia multi querunt me, sed non secundum scienciam sed propter mundum, alii plus iusto timent, alii nimis (0 Ab F Gh minus p t) presumunt, alii in consiliis suis superbiunt, ideo ego Deus, qui omnium operor salutem, facio quandoque illud prouenire, quod homo plus timet. The reading of the archetype is most likely to have been minus, represented by all the different branches of the tradition. On contextual grounds it is necessary to assume that people who, according to the archetype, minus presumunt, do some- thing that Jesus Christ in Birgitta's view dislikes. Since minus here would mean `too little' (or maybe 'not', cf. Hofmann-Szantyr 454 f.), then the verb presumere cannot be pejorative: it would, on the contrary, imply something good. How is, then, presumere used in the Revelations? The combination of presumere with an infinitive is rather usual and the meaning seems to be something like 'make bold to'. A pejorative sense is obvious in these cases, particularly when the clause is negative expressing a prohibition, e.g. Rev. VII 12: 38 vicarii vestri promittant sub iuramento, quod nichil facer e pr esumant contra iusticiam, VIII ii Scrip- meo fecit sibi apiarium, et impleuit eum melle suo and VI 31 B Sic et ego habeo os meum apertum ad offensionern tuam et turbationem, et numquam continerem eum a malo tuo ...; in both places eum was in Gh corrected into illud. My assumption that eum in the Serm. Arian. frag- ment is intended to be a neuter form is of course not contradicted by the preceding veords quod uteris, not quoted in Thes.: if quod, instead of an expected quo, really is the authentic reading, this indicates that indumentum was considered neuter. Whereas I did not find any example of indumentum used in the masculine gender (as distinguished from monumentuin, see Thes. 8, 1461, Ai and NG1 s.v., Norberg, Manuel 35), there is positive 28 f. ["ad exemplum vocis `titulus' evidence of the neuter indumentum in the Revelations, e.g. VII 7: i i non superflua sed necessaria indumenta, and Ex. 69: 3 aliud preciosum indumentum. Another argument against Önnerfors' rea- soning concerns the distribution of the reading qui in the tradition. In Önnerfors' opinion, this reading (given by I L w x P) may have been handed down from the archetype, but with regard to the relationship between the Rev. VII MSS. (see Bergh 32, 48, 50, 69, 112), an archetypal qui was then changed at least at seven points independently in the tradition, whereas an assumed que in the archetype implies only three different alterations to qui; evidently the reading of the archetype was, in fact, que.

VI 72 Filius loquitur: Due macule sunt in Secunda est quod parrochiales sacerdotes non audent absoluere ecclesia mea peccatores de omnibus peccatis suis occultis To sum up, in the cases discussed so far, presumere nearly always appears in a context with a pejorative meaning, even if it is not evident that the pejorative sense is attached to the verb presumere itself. Since our case int. 14: 25 does not exemplify either of the constructions examined, it is of course still more important to see what the verb means when it appears in an absolute function, either combined with an adverb, as in our case (minus in the archetype), or not. From the Revelations I did not list more than one case of the latter type, and here presumere is used in a clearly pejorative sense, rev. 6: 7 Dyabolus enim numquam dormit, quia temptacio occasio est perfeccionis, vt homo non pr esumat; the last clause is by Lunden 2, 282 rendered by "och hindrar henne från att bliva förmäten". The verb presumere was used in the same sense by Magister Mathias in Copia exemplorum, Cod. Ups. C 54 fol. I o7v confessor qui alienas temptaciones audit de seipso discretus sit ne pr esumat. In the Vulgate there is a parallel to this kind of presumere in Eccli. 32, 13 In medio magnatorum non pr aesumas, et ubi sunt senes non multum loquaris. An inducant uncertain case is given by Ep. Sol. 2 C (debet examinari, vtrum visiones nos ...) quod pr esumamus et confidamus de ... virtutibus nostris an quodhumiliemur: here it seems impossible to know, whether the expression de virtutibus nostris is to be combined with confidamus only or with presumamus et confidamus; if the latter alternative is correct, then the case should be ranged in the cases of presumere de se, considering the reflexive nostris.  83 absolute function of presumere combined with an adverb. This case is Rev. III 19 D Noli insuper confider e et presumere nimium, si quem dico amicum et seruum; Jesus Christ addresses Birgitta, warning her not to rely upon his words too much. As suggested above, there are in the Revelations some descriptions of men, who trusted too much in God's mercy, e.g. rev. 8: 4 ff., Add. IV 102 H, Rev. IV 107 D: confidence in God is something good, but man should not be allowed to trust in him too much, taking advantage of his indulgence. As regards our case it seems reasonable to draw the following conclusions from this examination of presumere. Only exceptionally presumere is used to denote something good, and in the absolute function it always stands for something bad.14 This justifies the assumption that the reading minus presumunt in our case is erroneous, since these words less likely imply something reprehensible. The paleo- graphically slight alteration into nimis presumunt," made in 0 Ab F Gh, gives a reading which is more consistent with the examined materia1.16 It should be ob- served that the only possible argument in favour of minus presumunt, viz. the inter- pretation "trust too little", which, in view of the material analysed above, would have required a construction with de (followed by God, Jesus Christ or Virgin Mary), can be used to support the reading nimis presumunt as well, as is evident presumere nimium, quoted above. from Rev. III 19 D Noli Another argument against the archetypal minus presumunt is given by the pre- ceding alii plus iusto timent: if minus presumunt were accepted, then the two clauses would express the same idea. However, the construction with the corresponding alii makes a certain contrast expected, and this contrast is excellently ex- alii nimis presumunt, i.e. an exaggerated fear is contrasted pressed by plus iusto timent with an undue self-confidence or arrogance. int. 15: 6-7 In (3 n the sixth questio of int. i 5 is given after the seventh, whereas the order in Gh corresponds to the one adopted in the present edition; in the respon- siones the order 6 7 is supported by all sources.
